I seek eases where all else has failed.
Young and middle-aged men and others, who
suffer from Nervous and Physical Debility,

who has failed to cure you. Cases that I accept

cured,
DO ANY OF THE FOLLOWING SYMPTOMS,

dition ? Do you suffer from Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
or other aches and pains ? Are you timid, nervous,
and forgetful with your mind continually dwelling on
die subject? Have you lost confidence in yourself,
and energy for business pursuits ? Are you subject
:o restless nights, broken sleep, dreams, palpitation
af the heart, bashfulncss, confusion of ideas, aversion
10 society, dizziness in the head, dimness of sight,
pimples on the face and back, and other desponding
symptoms? If you are afflicted, why further neglect
your health or future happiness, when by a single con-
sultation (which costs nothing) you can be placed on
the way to health, strength, and vigour?
All correspondence held sacred. A speedy
and perfect life cure is guaranteed in all cases
I undertake, where doubt exists the sufferer is
frankly informed.

r PHE directions contained in this little treatise will be
1 found of immense benefit to the sufferer ; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous

self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—Overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer _ from the following complaints, viz.: —Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Ur. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,

other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investif/ate his statements by verifying

READ THIS
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastorahsm.—Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. Ho thinks
the important features of the district are its grand .climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West Australia.
TEN POUND BUILDING SITES
at PERTH, WESTERN AUSTRALIA.

HE WOULDN’T PE NASTY.
“There being no objection to the preamble of the Bill, the Mayor, before putting it to the vote, said he was personally opposed to railways and tramways being taken out of the
hands of the Government and placed in the power of private people, but the city had received so very little consideration or remuneration from the Government for the use of its
streets, and the aldermen had so carefully considered the measure, that if they obtained the consent of the people he would have no objection to the adoption of a Bill of that
character.” — Mayor Burdekin on the Sydney Tramway Bill.
THE WHICH, BEING INTERPRETED

SATURDAY. JULY 26, 1890.
Will the Chinese Overrun the World?
We had at first proposed the question in a
different form, namely, “ Will the Chinese
conquer the world ?” On reflection, we sub-
stitute the heading as it now stands, because
it permits < f broader consideration. A race
may overrun the world, without waging war
upon it, just as effectually as though it re-
duced ail the peoples to subjection by force
of arms. Probably no European of this
generation has enjoyed opportunities for
basing an opinion relative to this question
equal to those which fell to the lot of the
late General Gordon — Chinese Gordon, as
he was styled—during the major portion of
his career. Mr. W. T. Stead affirms that
Gordon stated to him his conviction that
the Chinese are destined to overrun the whole
world. Whether Emin Pasha had been in-
doctrinated by < Job don with this belief ap-
pears doubtful, but it is clear that Gordon
had at least imbued his lieutenant with an
exalted appreciation of the efficiency of the
Chinese as agents of civilisation. Emin,
widely-travelled ( hicntalist as he is, has never
Sersonally had extended experience of the
hinese. It must therefore, in all reason-
able probability, be ascribed to his African
association with Gordon that his plan of
founding a civilised community in the
equatorial regions of Africa is based upon
the subjection of the negro by means
of a Chinese immigration under Ger-
man auspices and control. This project
can scarcely be reconciled with a par-
ticipation in Gordon’s belief as to
the destined domination of the globe
by the Chinese. Emin is a faithful Teuton
and Mould scarcely promote a migration
which would tend towards the ruin of his
own race by facilitating the realisation of
Gordon’s prophesy. Were the Mongolians
to break bounds and diffuse their myriads
over the face of the globe, it would materi-
ally assist the realisation of an universal
dominion were an acclimatised colony of
their countrymen established in the heart of
Africa ready to help them in that continent
from within. Moreover, one such experi-
ment as Emin has conceived would, if suc-
cessfully accomplished, have a strong ten-
dency to lead to other migrations, perhaps
not under German auguries, but spontaneous
or organised by the rulers of the Chinese
Empire. In order to realise the significance
which such a lodgment of Chinese might
assume, it is merely requisite to recall to
mind a peculiar suggestion which but a short
time ago created'some transient-emotion in-
Australia. A statement found currency that
the German Government contemplated ap-
propriating Queensland at the first fitting
opportunity. A material item in their cal-
culations to that end was alleged to be the
presence in that 'AustraHan state of a con-
siderable colony of German settlers. These
are ex-soldiers for the most part, as every
Gorman of a certain age must bo, in conse-
quence of the obligation, in the Father-
land, of universal military service. Aug-
mentation of the numbers already settled
in Queensland could be quietly and
steadily effected without exciting attention
or opposition, and when the hour for action
arrived, an expeditionary force would find an
army of co-nationalists ready to seize the
weapons and equipments brought for their
use—an army of which moreover the in-
dividual members would furnish guides
familiar with every nook of the territory,
with every road, every track, every position,
and with the local conditions, observance of
which might be essential to impart the fullest
efficiency to an invading force of new-chums.
This was prob ably a baseless rumour, or ait
least the echo of some abstract theorisings
which never really assumed the solidity of a
set purpose. It serves, however, to illus-
trate one of the possibilities of the future,
with respect to the chances of the world
being hereafter subjected to a new deluge—
a submergence of all Caucasian civilisation
under a flood of Chinese.
* # #
The Mongolian is the sole race respecting
which such a conception of destiny could be
formed. The Caucasian is divided into a
score of nationalities each antipathetic to
the other, controlled by governments
mutually antagonistic, actuated by powerful
repulsions, dispersed over the face of the
earth, and at present incapable of any com-
bination for their common interest. The
Mongolians, on the contrary, have the bulk
of their race concentrated in one compara-
tively compact territory, controlled by a
single and despotic ruler. For 2000 years
they have been repairing the depletion
in their numbers consequent upon a
colossal migration which at that time
occurred. Their energies have been com-
pressed within the narrow limits of their
native land, while the Caucasians have been
diffusing their peoples in vast colonisations
of regions previously thinly peopled, or
occupied only by weak and barbarous races,
or by multitudinous but effete relics of
expiring civilisations such as the Aztec. The
universal inspiration of movement has, how-
ever, of late years affected in some degree,
at least, the fringes of the Chinese popula-
tion. The Malayan Archipelago and the
auriferous regions of Western America and
of Australia have felt their presence. In
small communities, such as the Friendly
Islands, their numbers press upon the
original peoples, and threaten to displace
them. The United States have formally
repelled these emigrants, and our own thin
populations have ventured to enact prohibi-
tive legislation. These proceedings, while
operating as a temporary check, appear, from
many indications, to have been effectual in
an adverse direction. They have aroused
the attention of the Chinese Government,
hitherto almost unconscious of the outward
movement of infinitesimal fractions of its
subjects, to tlie evidences of pressure upon
the home space, and to the existence of
delectable regions without. They have
further affronted the haughty spirit of high
and mighty rulers who have been accustomed
to regard all the outer world as constituted
of tributary kingdoms and inferior peoples.
It is but recently that a Chinese ambassador
conveyed to the Court of St. James a per-
emptory demand from his sovereign, that to
the Chinese people should be extended the
same privileges, throughout the British
dominions, as are extended to the subjects
of any other foreign potentate.
# # #
It is not, however, our design to consider
too minutely the possible consequences of
particular instances of contact. The full
significance of the probabilities which impend
in the future may, we conceive, he best appre-
ciated by considerations of a broader kind. By
wide generalisation certain deductions become
apparent. The estimated population of the
world is at the present time, in round num-
bers, 1,45(1,000,000. The inhabitants of the
Chinese Empire make up no less than
450,000,000 of that total. Racial vanity
has of late years induced various speculative
writers to venture the prediction that at a
future time not exceedingly remote, English-
speaking people will dominate the earth. On
how poor a basis this presumption rests can
be perceived Ivy a simple comparison of
figures. At the present moment there are
in the whole world about 12(5 000,000 of
English-speaking people. There are about
four times as many Chinese consolidated in
one corner of Asia.
* * #
If the chances of war be eliminated from
the question, it becomes simple enough.
Not much longer, can it lie supposed, will
the Chinese be able, even if willing, to avoid
intimate associations with the rest of the
world’s population. 4he Mongolian race
must cither extinguish all other races,
entirely or partially, or it must blend with
them. If the fusion bo peaceful, the future
inhabitant of the world will be more of a
Chinese than of any other typo. Since the
Mongolians constitute one-third, roughly
speaking, of the world’s population, the
progeny of a world-wide fusion would be in
type and featui’e one-third Chinese, about
one-sixth European, and the balance, in
varying small proportions, Indo-Caucasian,
negro, and so forth. But it would not be
rational, in view' of the present temper,
habits and tendencies of .nations and races,
to ant icipate a pacific fusion. The antipa-
thies lo be overcome are (on potent, the
combative' instinct still too predominant.
The Chinese will have to conquer or to be
conquered. Should they be conquered, yet
will their type enter into the man of the
future. Despite the utmost devastation and
slaughter conceivable as accompanying the
most savage struggle to which nations in-
spired by modern European civilisation
could by any possibility descend, the women
would be spaced, and of the two-and-a-half
hundred millions of males it is inconceivable
that a tithe would be extinguished. The con-
querors in such case would, as has happened
previously after a like convulsion, become
after a few generations modified into some-
thing of the mould of the people they had
subdued. And not modified in a physical
sense only. When the mighty fabric of the
Roman Empire of the West was over-
whelmed barbarous masses which
fell upon it, there emerged from beneath the
ruins a new creation of men, in whom the
old civilisation wrought towards its renais-
sance.
# * *
If, on the other hand, history should re-
peat itself, and China should break out in a
fresh eruption of warlike hordes, the almost
total obliteration of the European type would
be a not impossible consequence. The
Oriental system of warfare has lost none of
its devastating ferocity since Baber erected
pyramids of skulls as mementos of the de-
feat of an array and the extermination of a
people. J enghis K han, when he had subdued
the northern provinces of China itself, de-
liberately intended to exterminate the entire
population, in order to make the tract avail-
able for pasturage. The same Oriental con-
queror, on making himself master of four
great cities, gloried in the precision of the
record which handed down to posterity the
account of a slaughter of four million three
hundred and forty-seven thousand people.
Policy sometimes restrained ferocity, and
life was occasionally granted to such
suitable male prisoners as availed them-
selves of “freedom of contract” to
enlist in the troops of the conqueror.
Timour, at Delhi, massacred 100,000 pri-
soners because some had been seen to smile
on the approach of an army of their com-
patriots. Timour, on storming Ispahan,
raised a tower of 70,000 human skulls, and
on repressing a revolt at Bagdad employed
90,000 to erect a similar trophy. It should
not, therefore, be difficult to conceive how
nearly a Mongolian eruption, should it prove
successful, would approach to a process of
extermination. The scope of such an out-
burst may be imagined by reference to the
fact, narrated by Gibbon, that the hordes of
Attila, on entering Europe, swept the
whole breadth of the continent from above
500 miles from the Euxine to the Adriatic.
War, indeed, among any people, is but
organised savagery. The savage reprisals
by the Germans on the wretched inhabitants
of the petty town of Bazeilles, in revenge
for some shots fired from the houses, is still
recent in the memory of this generation.
“ Our men swore dreadfully in Flanders ”
and acted as dreadfully as they swore. The
“ Spanish Fury ” and the “French Fury”
at Antwerp during the struggle of the
Netherlands for independence fell little
short of Oriental ferocity. As recently
as 1820 the exploits of British soldiers in
America needed but little exascerbation to
rival the worst records. At New' Orleans
the General-in-Command issued as pass-
word for the day of an intended assault the
atrocious hint, “Booty and beauty,” and
when, on the march to Washington, the
soldiers, at the village of New Hampstead,
violated the women, their commander made
shift to excuse himself by alleging that as
old Peninsular campaigners they had acquired
habits of a regretable character.
* * *
It appears, then, that whether the process
be pacific or warlike, the Mongolian cannot
fail to impress himself upon the future popula-
tion of the world, to a degree which must be
regarded as nearly equivalent, even in the
former case, to overrunning it, and as in the
latter case tantamount to monopolising it.
There lacks for the more terrific event an
augmentation of motive forces already in
operation. The torrent of Mongolians
who obliterated the Roman Empire re-
ceived its first impulse from a convul-
sion in China. A stupendous remnant
of a vast early population, the Hiong Nu,
fleeing before a conqueror,, thrust before
them races more barbarous than themselves
and their separate and ultimately mingled
masses submerged European civilization from
the banks of-the Rhine and Danube to the
southernmost cape of what is now Spain.
Much consolation is sometimes pretended
to be found in the circumstances that
modern war partakes largely of the
quality of a science, and its 'materiel
demands for its construction and removal
mechanical processes and accomplishment's
which China as yet has not adopted. But
this is slender comfort in an age when the
very savages of Polynesia fire upon boats’
crews with magazine rifles, when African
kings insist upon having Gatling and Maxim
guns when complimentary gifts are in ques-
tion, and when our commercial morality is
so debased, that the most pious merchant
shuts his eyes, his ears, and his conscience
while his agents barter rum, rifles, and Bibles
in mixed assortments for ivory and gold-dust,
copra and pearlshell, and when, if two
nations are blowing each other’s people to
atoms, the traders of a third are eagerly
hovering round with explosives to sell.
Murder and Snuffle.
The bu.T'en of too much superfluous preacher
seems to have settled down heavily on New
Guinea. The missionary and the cannibal divide
the colony between them, and the rest of the
world is rigidly excluded. No bad Australian
miner is allowed to tread these shores if the utmost
exertions of the reverend shepherd can keep him
out. No low, degraded settler can buy or lease
land,- for the land all belongs either to the coloured
brother or to the Church, and the Church wants
all that the coloured brother can be induced to
part with for the good of Ids soul. Nobody trades,
there if the spreader of good tidings can help it,
for that person feels himself equal to all the trad-
ing requirements of the country; and, moreover,
it is claimed that the white man is a demoralised
individual, whose presence is calculated to pollute
the native, and prevent him subscribing his yam
to the great cause of Terewth. The imported
pastor, in fact, runs the entire Administration,
and in the glowing reports which he issues
from time to time he foreshadows the
coming of a Golden Age, when through
the exclusion of everybody except himself—
the eater of human flesh shall be reformed and
sing hymns beneath his palm-tree, and the savage
with the spiked club and the old squashed hat
shall take up a collection, while a few words of
Christian exhortation are offered by a person with
a fish-bone stuck through his.nose. New Guinea,
in that future period, is to' be an idyllic State,
where the simple, naked believer will wallow in
Sunday-school, and regard his missionary with a
profound and holy veneration; and as the first
step towards this millennium Australia pays all
the expenses of a Government whose chief busi-
ness it is to exclude Australians from the country,
lest their nefarious presence shall infect the shep-
herd and his flock.
* * *
There is a good deal of food for reflection in the
political system of New Guinea. If the white
man is so hopelessly degraded that his very exis-
tence in the island threatens to demoralise the
prize heathen of that locality, it seems strange
that the missionary should not rather devote
himself to elevating the miserable Caucasian so as
to render him fit to associate with the undressed
savage who goes round and kills his fellow-man
with a spear, and then eats him after he is dead
for fear he should go bad if left lying about. If
no white man, except the missionary himself, is
capable of mixing with the illiterate aboriginal
without dragging that unhappy individual down-
wards in the social scale, it naturally follows that
the whole white race, except the said missionary,
is on the high-road to perdition, and if missionaries
were subject to the ordinary rules of logic, it
would be hard to explain why the only virtuous
man in a perishing community should thus aban-
don his lost countrymen to hunt after souls and
copra in New' Guinea. The advent of the Cau-
casian in this new land, it is claimed, would be
the beginning of a gradual process of murder and
extermination, but it is this same homicidal
Caucasian who pays the preacher’s salary, and
under these painful conditions it would be a good
and holy thing for that preacher to give up his
high-priced billet, and devote himself rather to
educating the people who send the Gospel to the
heathen, so that they could be trusted within
gunshot of the tribes whose souls they yearn after.
As he shows no desire to attempt anything of this
sort, however, there seems some slight reason to
believe that his objection to any intrusion except
his own is based on the fear that there may not be
enough trade to go round.
# * *
British colonisation is a process of three stages.
The first consists in snuffling over the unhappy
native, the second in murdering him by various
means, and the third in snuffling over him again
after he is dead. Other nations murder without
the snuffle, and as no power yet discovered seems
sufficient to preserve, a feeble and decaying race
from extermination we would prefer the foreign
method. The snuffle is miserably incongruous.
Even the scanty intelligence of the missionary is
sufficient to tell him that the incessant restless-
ness of the Anglo-Saxon will lead him to overrun
New Guinea as he has overrun so many other
countries in the past, and even the limited reason-
ing powers of the preacher must tell him that his
picture of an Arcadian church on the islands is
the emptiest of shams. He has already failed
miserably in every country on earth—including
his own—and every successive failure is worse than
the last. The shoeless believer who is to sing
hymns under the palm-tree never even starts the
first verse. The person with the club and the
squashed hat who is to be converted and
carry round the plate doesn’t fulfill his
mission worth a cent, because he is away
eating a boiled child up on his native hills. The
reclaimed exhorter witli the fishbone stuck
through his nose never exhorts, and the reason is
that he is out killing somebody, or whaling his
wife with a bunch of brambles. But if everything
else fails the missionary generally gets far enough
ahead to secure the best lands before the secular
invasion begins, and he is doing this in New
Guinea to the utmost of his ability. After that
the heathen must take his chance, and it is
generally a bad one. But if, as is most probable,
the aboriginal of New Guinea is to go the way of
the rest, then for heaven’s sake let it be acknow-
ledged openly that he was killed off because he
was in the road ; and let none of the people who
plundered him, from the missionary downwards,
make a hollow show of yearning after him when
he is dead. For this yearning business lias
been altogether played out, for at least a half a
century.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Liberty of the Subject.
The Victorian Assembly is just now debating a
Factories and Shops Act Amendment Fill in-
tended to overrule certain municipal bye-laws, In
right of which Melbourne tradesmen are enabled
to evade, in a small measure, the seven o’clock
closing rule sanctioned by the Legislature some
four or fne years back. Objections, honest
enough and seemingly of considerable weight, are
urged against the “Curfew clause, ' as Tories have
trickily described it, said Tories choosing to forget
—if they ever knew that the Conqueror's enact-
ment deprived toilers of the very opportunities
for recreation which the Victorian law would
secure for them, The main argument against the
new Bill is that suburban shopkeepers must
thereby suffer a loss of trade, or even lie eom-
pelle l~to tfetfre from tlieif pre-ent occupations,
for the benefit of the large town houses.
Pathetic word-pictures of the struggling widow,
who supports a large family by retailing
small, fly-blown goods until a comparatively late
hour, are submitted to the notice of the colony by
ledalaters supplying their daily needs from “the
wholesale.” The anomaly of street-arabs being
allowed to vend matches in the streets until mid-


• ht is denounced on the false assumption that
n -ud urchins are a source of commercial injury to
the widowed keeper of a Berlin-woolshop. An-
other sapient contention is that, even if assistants
be allowed to leave at seven o’clock, “there
should be nothing to prevent their employer keep-
• 0. ope n, if he so choose, with the aid of his own
family ” which idea is calculated to encourage the
raising of shopkeeper families, and confer mono-
uolies u pon retail traders who have their quivers full
of them. It is fair to admit that no legislator has
o far attempted to prove that a strict carrying out
of Reakin’s Early Closing Act would work to the
disadvantage of any single industry in the aggre-
gate There are limits even to the bosh talked
bv petty-minded politicians. Admitted that a
very considerable number of shopkeepers stand to
lose custom, more or less, by the philanthropic
curfew, and that a few melancholy hangers-on to
the skirts of commerce would be forced to
relinquish their feeble grip. The commonwealth
must inevitably benefit by a law which reduces
the hours of labour for shop-assistants—a prover-
bially helpless class, by the way—tends to the
condensation of trade, forces the procrastinating
housewife to “get in her few things” within
the hours hitherto devoted to yarning across
the fence, and, most important of all, compels a
number of able-bodied non-producers to earn their
bread and cheese in employment more Useful than
that of waiting for customers in the little back
parlor, like spiders watching for flies. A real
sting lurked in Napoleon’s taunt that the Eng-
lish were a nation of shopkeepers. Too much
keeping of shop is not good for a nation. In all
industrial trades the desirability of diminishing
the ranks of middlemen is recognised, and although
the shopkeeper is an absolutely necessary middle-
man he is the last man in the country whose
profits should be protected at the expense of the
“liberty of the subject.” And the liberty of the
subject should commence at 7 p.m. at the very
latest when possible.

The Day of Rest.
THE old Sunday observance question—that hoary
problem which first started when the primeval
patriarch nefariously navigated the Ark on the
day of rest —is again in full blast in Melbourne.
For years and years the inhabitant of that priest-
ridden, parson-sodden city has loafed wearily in
the gutters on the Sabbath day, and dejectedly
fitted his feet into the cracks of the pavement, and
surreptitiously shuffled round to the hack door of
the pub. and implored the landlord for a drink,
and has trailed dejectedly through the empty
parks or lounged profanely along that infamous
waste which forms the shore of Hobson’s Bay;
and for the good of his soul all rational recreation
has been denied him. The proposal to open the
public libraries, museums, and art-galleries has
been mooted a hundred times, and as often it has
been suppressed, lest these opportunities for
mental improvement should draw off the attend-
ance at the tabernacle, where the preacher shakes
up the old dry bones of dogma or snuffles with a
dolorous hum of wailing and lamentation over his
dead brother—the extinct Pharisee of the past.
The Daily Telegraph, in particular, _as _ the
voice of clerical intolerance, has lifted up its inky
dirge over the sinfulness of a scheme whereby
some dozen officials would be required to break
the Sabbath and knock the pieces about for the
public convenience; and when a great public
demonstration was held last week in defence of
the proposed innovation, and a few of the more
advanced clergymen appeared to support the “ un-
clean thing,” the language of that pious organ
was like unto the language of the indignant prize-
fighter, or the angry “drunk” who lifts up his
voice at the corner. It is true, of course, that the
tramway employes have to labour seven days in
the week in order to carry the languid saint to
church and back again ; and that the policeman is
required to break the commandment lest some
stray rowdy should hit the church in the eye on
the Sabbath ; and that the hired soprano in the
fashionable choir earns filthy lucre by toiling up
to G, and higher if she can manage it, with a view
to attracting the opera-loving sinner, and the
patron of the leg-show, within reach of the collec-
tion ; and the church-bell soloist makes the day
hideous at a fixed salary; and the par-
son follows his worldly avocation for money,
and if there is no money forthcoming
the flock may go and rave in Gehenna —all these
things do not count. Even the trains run on
Sunday, and fetch the suburban believer and the
outlying convert —but that also is a matter of no
importance. But the proposition that libraries
and art-galleries should be opened on the only
day when the people who pay for these institu-
tions have time to make use of them, has caused
the solitary white hair of the Melbourne parson to
assume a vertical position from grief and horror.
If the average preacher does not know how to
make himself interesting enough for the public to
care to listen to him, then the public must loaf
along the gutter and smoke its offensive short pipe
on the pavement as of old, and if it wants to
get access to any amusement which is better
and more, rational than leaning up against the
lamp-post] at|the corner, it is to be duly arrested
and fined according ti the Act of that pious
monarch who was a father to his people 200 years
ago—or, at least, who was a father to as many
of them as any one white man could well
manage to be. May his name be held in reverence,
for he was a good and holy potentate, who drank
liberally and cursed profusely, arid broke all the
commandments he had ever heard of; and when
he died of lush and other worldly indulgences he
left a Sabbath Observance Act behind him for the
benefit of his posterity—which was very numerous
and has drawn its pension regularly ever since.

The Crown in a Mess.
Since the Premier Building Association “trouble”
began to take the shape of a grave political scandal,
The Bulletin has faithfully foretold the develop-
ment of events in that most unsavoury connection.
It was indeed easy work to read the future up to a
time which has brought the Gillies Government
face to face with a phenomenal difficulty. Through-
out the piece The Bulletin has plainly and simply
declared that the Crown would have to take over
the prosecution from Colonel Templeton, and
would take it over with an earnest intention of
not going straight against its old friends and
political second cousins. “It must have Purves,”
we observed in the former of two articles,
headed “Watching the Crown.” The Crown
doesn’t want Purves, however. How can that
emblem of law and justice be in touch with a
dreadful man who undertakes to prove conspiracy
against a defendant who received letters full of
sympathy and “butter” from the Premier, before
the trial commenced? Pshaw ! Didn’t one or
more of the defendants honour the Speaker by
sharing his private box at Melbourne Opera House
after the trial had commenced ? Why, certainly.
Let us take the “official ” leaning towards these in-
fluential defendants for granted. Everybody in
Melbourne knows all about it, and there is no-
thing more sinfully funny than to watch the big
city pot—himself on good terms with officialism—
as he says, “I don’t think anything will come of
this prosecution, do you?’’ Before an honest man he
droops his eyelids after firing off the remark, to the
chum who isn’t quite so honest he winks solemnly,
pompously, not to say apoplectically. The wink
of the c xnmercial Podsnap is as impressive as a
royal funeral. But this by the way. Mr.
Attorney-General Righteous Wrixon has not
been discreet in his playing of the Crown game.
He has shown his hand in the second round, not
stealthily nor by accident, but with the ostenta-
tion characteristic of a man who broke exit into a
Bill at the first whisper of a Sunday newspaper in
Victoria. Colonel Templeton swore two charges
against the Premier Association directors. The
first is now being heard (or rather, wasn’t being
heard at time of writing), and the second —a
charge of appropriating share premiums to them-
selves—would naturally follow in due course. Mr.
Wrixon announces his intention to take counsel’s
opinion as to whether ho will allow that second
charge (that awkward, simple, straightforward
charge, which cannot be buried in a mass of evi-
dence and legal dust) to lapse, die, disappear.
Mr. Wrixon, already lias the opinion of the
counsel he unwillingly took over from Colonel
Templeton, and which include the appalling Q.C.
Purves. It was on their advice that the second
charge was booked. Their opinion does not satisfy
the Crown, so Mr. Wrixon has the smug imper-
tinence to pick Dr. Madden, a barrister now ap-
pearing for one of the defendants, as the head of
a tribunal which shall decide whether the slrares-
charge ought to be dropped'! ! The high profes-
sional reputation of Dr. Madden is out of
harmony, to our way of thinking, with his willing-
ness to sit in judgment on his own side. On the
other hand, the professional reputation of Messrs.
Purves, Coldham, &c., will not permit them to be
set at naught by anon-practising Attorney-General.
Purves, in particular, is entire master of the
situation in a sentimental sense, he being the
Queen's counsel, whose opinion the Crown is now ,
seeking to over-rule. The scandalous stupidity of
Mr. Wrixon has-fortunately put him right in the
mud he made for himself. The Crown counsel
have vehemently re-affirmed their decision that
the second charge against the Crown’s friends is
good ; further, that if the Crown doesn't prosecute
Colonel Templeton shall, and, finally, that they
will stand by the Colonel, who has fought against
political and Press intrigue single-handed, and
conduct the prosecution for love, honour and
glory. , The men stand pledged to themselves to
get somebody convicted, and _ the Victorian
Ministry—God bless ’em!—are in a distressing
quandary.

Saint Dailey.
The proposal to erect a memorial window to
Mr. W. B. Dalley, Q. 0., in St. Mary’s R.C.
Cathedral, Sydney, may be well-intentioned, but
has not been prudently conceived. Mr. Dalley
has had a tablet erected in St. Paul’s, in London,
but that building is not peculiarly a religious one.
Any man who, by powder, sword, or lying, adds a
slice of territory to the British dominion can look
forward to a niche in that house of worship, which
is but the prayer-room of a pawnshop. Any man
who gives Crown or Church a bagful of the money
lie sweated out of the poor or harvested from their
folly, can command a commemorative space on wall
or window. That church is a national hoarding
for the gratification of advertising maniacs, and
colossal wealth or colossal glory may dispute within
it pride of place and promineriqeof position with the
Ten Commandments or the emblems of Deicide.
The Australian St. Mary’s is not that—and the
Australian public who are called upon to subscribe
on this occasion should pause before they allow a
little St. Paul’s to be founded in their midst. Is
it Dalley’s religious character that calls for this
memorial window ? Dare a Roman Catholic
bricklayer who married outside the laws of his
church, and who lived the full length of his mar-
ried life in defiance of these laws, arid who only
after his wife’s death returned to the fold, look
forward to a memorial window in a Roman Catholic
cathedral church ? Dare he look forward to an
orthodox grave ? Deniehy did not so distinctly
and publicly reject ecclesiastical authority, and
the Roman Catholic Church left him to be buried
as a dog. Deniehy was not approved and extolled,
by Royalty or its associates, and down ac-
cordingly he was dumped as a dog, and
the church turned its. nose up and passed by,
sniffing the air of abstract virtue. Dalley has
been approved by Royalty and glorified by Pro-
testant St. Paul’s, and now a movement lumbers
along to establish him as a Roman Catholic saint.
Of course a man may repent of misdeeds, and
repentance welds together the broken links in
virtue’s chain. But who, we again ask, dare
maintain that Dalley would have ecclesiastically
repented had his wife survived ? Perhaps is the
only word which the observer of human pheno-
mena can apply to such a situation. His
brother-in-law, Sir James Martin, died unrecon-
ciled to the faith, his wife surviving. Dalley
died reconciled—-his wife dead. Had Dalley’s
wife survived, what is the rational hypothesis ?
Sir James Martin never adopted Church of
England faith. He was a Roman Catholic of a
firm Roman Catholic stock, of spontaneous
Roman Catholic sentiment, and, his wife alive,
he died as, in marriage, he had lived. But
although he was not an Anglican, Bishop Barry
went out and buried him with Anglican religious
rites. Why? Because he was a front man, a
“Sir,” a person whose standing when alive and
■ whose name wheri dead, were grateful to caste-
respectability. The same sentiment operates in
Dalley’s case. The Roman Catholic Church in
Australia has not had too many eminent adherents.
It is determined, therefore, to preserve its Dalley.
He was a Privy Councillor, a Right Honorable,
and a memorial tablet or window in St. Mary’s
will be an evidence to bricklayers and servant-
girls in the back pews that even distinguished men
believe in the faith preached from the. pulpit.
But Biddy must not forget that if she marries a
Protestant according to Protestant rites, she stands
a small show of getting a tablet; and Murphy,
the bricklayer, if he marries a Protestant woman,
will almost certainly be refused church burial.
Saint Dalley will have the privilege of looking
through a stained-glass window and super-
vising ceremonies generally in St. Mary’s, not
because he was religiously fervent, but because
he was politically and socially a success.
Dalley helped to butcher native Soudanese,
“rightly struggling to be free.” His advocacy
of this butchery, and that alone, gave him his
niche in St. Paul’s Cathedral. He was put there,
without any regard to, his religion. He was re-
garded simply as a useful tool in the hands of
“ Monarchy, of Royalty and he was on that ac-
count emblazoned. Are the Roman Catholics of
New South Wales who, in the main, are opposed
to the designs of Monarchy, Royalty, to back
up the theatrical manoeuvres of the Throne-
adoring experts of St. Paul’s? That is what the
whole thing comes to. As a Roman Catholic,
Dalley was of indifferent or no repute. As a
loyalist, a Royalist, his reputation was high. The
point then is, What is this window in St. Mary’s
to represent? Is it the butcher of the Soudan, or
the devout son of the Roman Church ? But
he was not the latter while home influence exerted
itself, and as the former his memory is not revered
by nine-tenths of the people of Australia. Mr.
Dalley’s nature was loveable, generous, sympa-
thetic. He was a wit; he was a poet, though he
sang but little. But he was not a saint. In
Australia, it rests with two classes only to pay
honour to his memory—those who were his friends
or beneficial res, and those who agreed with him as
an Imperialist.

Pierre Douar, a French convict, escaped from
Pentridge prison a few months ago, and win n re-
captured was sentenced to two years in irons.
Eyery evening at six o’clock he was locked up
alone in a cell where he had to stay in total dark-
ness from eight o'clock until six next morning.
When the warder looked in on last Thursday at
six a.m. lie beheld the dead body of Douar, who
had strangled himself in the night with the strap
by which his fetters were held up while he shuffled
along. Douar was a scoundrel, but a brave
scoundrel. That such hideous, useless and
alicnising penalties should have been inflicted on
the better side of his nature, his love of liberty,
man’s noblest instinct,-is revolting to the human
mind. How many men are driven mad by the
illogical and brutal solitary system it would be
hard to tell. How many men have been reformed
by the solitary system ? Some convicts can lie
down on hard boards in Cimmerian darkness and
sleep like pigs. For other men ten hours in a dark
cell, night after night, means insanity. “ I object
to flogging,” said Premier Parkes, not long ago,
“ because it is a most unequal punishment.” And
how unequal is solitary confinement, the Pro-
crustean bed, on which the law and the judges
stretch all men alike —those whose offences are the
result of calculating dogged brutality, and those
whose crimes have come from outside circum-
stances acting on nervous excitability ?
The action of the Sydney' ’Bus Co. in, to put
it mildly, discouraging the formation of a ’bus-
drivers’trades-union, was, from a ’Bus Co. p hit
of view, a deplorable tactical mistake. ' “ We
are,” praeticafly announce the company, “a lot of
capitalists who hate tradesumions, and we want
organised Labour to help us in getting control of
the Government tramways.” After this, one may
he sure that the New South Wales Trades and
Labour Council will help along the tram-sale pro-
ject by every means in its power. If sucli things
be done in the green-leaf what shall be done in
the dry? How helpless are the Melbourne tram-
employes in the hands of the local Tram and ’Bus
Co., a powerful corporation consisting to no small
extent of the very men who are endeavouring to
get the Sydney tramways - into their own hands !
The employes curse and mutter,: but—bah ! what
does the Company care? It has its servants
under the whip—it has on its books the names of
an army of applicants for employment whom it
has trained for its service and bn whom it can rely
to supply at a day’s notice the place of every man
Jack to whom it pays wages. The Sydney work-
men must bring the Sydney Tram and ’Bus Com-
pany’s employes, to the very last man and boy,
into the Labour Federation ere the Company
obtain the control of the city streets. If they
don’t they will in the near future be simply
laughed at by the furthest-reaching and most
energetic lot of big capitalists ever banded together
in Australia.

There is one thing which nobody appears to
take into account in dealing with the Australian
grog-bill question, and that is the still active
spirituous influence of the amiable Mr. Eli John-
son, who visited this country a few years ago. Eli
was a temperance-reformer, so he said, and one of
the ways in which he used to show the folly of
spirit-drinking was this ; Standing on the tailboard
Of his cart with a tumbler containing an ounce or
two of white spirit in one hand and holding aloft
a little phial of coloured liquid in the other, he
would say—“ Now, my dear friends, I am going-
to make for you some rum.” After dropping a
■ little of the contents of the phial into the glass
and stirring up the contents thereof, he would
pass it round to be tasted, and, to do him credit,
everybody always pronounced - the mixture to be
most excellent rum. And so he went through the
whole gamut of gin, brandy, and so on. Of course,
Eli’s little jugglery tended to strengthen the faith
of the just who needed not repentance, but it.cut
two ways all the, same. Not a few of the low
publicans and sly-grog-sellers interviewed him and
acquired his secret—for, it is presumed, a con-
sideration. And that is possibly what is the
matter with the N.S.W. grog-revenue.
Mr. Joseph Mitchell, M.P., who has been
away in England for six months on private busi-
ness, and has during his absence drawn £l5O
“ Parliamentary allowance” from the New South
Wales Treasury, no doubt thinks he made a very
smart reply to a miner, a constituent of his, who
grumbles at Mr. Mitchell’s having taken pay for
work which he has neglected to do. “As ‘Miner,’”
he says, “only counts one, I find his proportion
of the said expense, if the whole amount be divided
among the population, is practically a third of a
penny. Enclosed please find a penny for him” —and
so oh. Now, supposing Mr. Mitchell had drawn
twenty times £l5O, or £3OOO, while away in Europe
it would, according to the same process of reason-
ing, only have cost the country under 7d. ahead,
and 7d., being only a little over the price of two
cheap drinks, is surely not worth talking about.
But there are such things as principles, Mr. Mit-
chell. When £5O is stolen from the public
Treasury it means a loss of only one-ninth of a
penny to each inhabitant of New South Wales,
yet the New South Wales Government puts into
gaol for perhaps three years the young man who
got the £5O. The Bulletin, of course, imputes
nothing dishonest to Mr. M itchell in this matter,
but it cannot help remarking that he has what the
Americans call “cast-iron gall.”

activity, a Miss Helen P. Phillips, in the inevi-
table postscript which presumably contains the
gist of her argument, says ;
I presume a “Man ” has never heard of Miss Thompson,
Florence Nightingale, Queen Philippa, Queen Mar-
garet, Mrs. Garrett Anderson, and a host of others.
Up to now women do not seem to know the names
of the greatest of their sex ; it is almost a wonder
that the lady quoted did not instance Queen Vic-
toria, the impress Eugenie, Helen of Troy, and
Mrs. Doctor Potts, She omits altogether to
mention “George Eliot,” Georges Sand, and
Harriet Marttneac (a most masculine intellect)
and drags in Miss Thompson, who, only great
because she has been smiled on by royalty, is not
fit to mix colours for Rosa Bonheur The men-
tion of Mrs. Garrett Anderson, notably chiefly
because her doggedness procured for her an M.D.-
ship, and Florence Nightingale, great only l»-
cause of her extreme womanliness, and certain
queens (how your ordinary woman worships a
title!) proves that the lady, like many other people,
has not the faintest idea of what constitutes
greatness. Why should Florence Nightingale
be set up as an idol, while Sophia Perovskaia
(who is not respectable because she was hanged
recently) is passed over? What is Mrs. Garrett
Anderson compared with Mary Somerville ?
A Story of a Governor.
How I Beat the Baringhup Express.
Glancing through this book of Sir George Bowen s
(which, by the way, isn’t his book at all), I am
reminded of several events in that genial old
humbug’s Victorian viceregal career which his
chronicler has omitted to set down, One in
particular I will not readily forget, It was my duty
for several years to follow Sir George in his many
pilgrimages through the Victorian country dis-
tricts, and to duly and faithfully record his weighty
utterances. After a while this labour became to
me mere child’s play, as I knew beforehand pretty
well what His Excellency would say. Thus,
if lie addressed a community of miners, say at
Sandhurst, lie invariably made the Dignity of
Labour the burden of his speech, and lugged
in Bobby Burns and his assertion that “a
man’s a man for a’ that.” On the contrary,
amongst the bucolics of Bungaree, Oliver Gold-
smith and his “bold peasantry—their country’s
prid“,” supplanted the bard cf Scotland com-
pletely. Sir George was in great form at an
agricultural show at Baringhup so long ago that I
cannot remember the year. And I was there, too,
of course, to “do” the show, and the Governor’s
bold peasantry, and after it was over preparations
were made for a start by special train for Mary-
borough, nine miles distant. Sir George was to
meet the miners of Maryborough that same even-
ing, and I had to be in Maryborough also to do
the man who was a man for a’ that after Governor
Bowen had done with him. There was no pos-
sibilty of reaching Maryborough from Baringhup
except by the special train, consisting of engine
and ribbons, a most sumptuous saloon-carriage
(capable of seating comfortably 50 persons), and a
waggon to impart an artistic and comforting
finish to the whole. As soon as Her
Majesty’s representative—with suite of three
persons—had entered the saloon I prepared
to follow (a man being a man for a’ that), but
the horrified guard repulsed me rudely, and
directed my attention to the waggon, to which he
led me, opening the door of that same. On look-
ing in I observed hampers, wines—in short, as
much provisions and drinkables as would supply
the crew of the Orlando for a week. Stranger
still, there were seated in the waggon (and quite
close, you may be sure, to the liquors) three tall,
large men, with that seedy, shabby-genteel
clothing which so generally adorns the scribe in
every generation of the world’s history. These
gentlemen, I afterwards learned, were the repre-
sentatives of the Melbourne dailies. They had a
trifle more sense than I had then, for the journey
was pleasant and the liquor good. As for me, my
pride, as well as my sense, of eongruity,_ was
offended at the arrangement made, and I declined
to enter the waggon. “All right,” said the
guard ; a shrill blast of a whistle—phew
and in an instant there was I alone on
the platform and the train almost out of'
sight. There was no conveyance to follow; so
I set out to tramp the journey. It was 1a.m.
when I reached Maryborough : the town was as
still as the grave, and I fancied I could hear
Bowen snoring soundly as I passed the M‘Ivor hotel.
The only persons about were the Maryborough
Advertiser people-and—were not they about in
earnest. Where had I been—what had detained me ?
Why hadn't I stopped away altogether; for the
Governor had taken the miners by storm with a
most jovial speech. Well, I said very little, but
assured them it was all right, and set to work in
my room. 1 had the copy taken away by the printer
as fast as I filled it, and presently I had to describe
the express journey ; the waving corn (corn always
waves when a Governor drives past) I told how
an ignorant democratic bull rushed towards the
railway fence as the train came along, but retired
with a bow on perceiving Majesty represented by
this bland and gallant gentleman. And soon the
aspect of the whole country changed, and boulders;
and shafts and whips and whims and a general air
of desolation took the place of luxuriant pastures,
for now the bold peasantry was in the rear, and
the man that was a man for a’ that was forninst
us. Then came the reception and the speeches.
The Mayor of Maryborough happened to be an
undertaker that year. I knew what an under-
taker would say on such an occasion, and I wrote
it down. His Excellency? The thing was as easy
as lying ! I trotted out the bold peasantry (already
set up) and now I galloped forth the Miner,
the Toiler, the Worker, the man who was,
indeed, for a’ that—a Man ! Nor was Bobby
Burns omitted. I quoted—I mean Sir George
Bowen quoted—two full verses of the poet who
first in all Britain sang of the equality of man and
the dignity of labour. Other speeches followed,
and the report closed amidst general rejoicings.
Generally commended was that able resume of the
gubernatorial visit. I made one mistake. I stated
that the train arrived at Maryborough ten minutes
before it actually did. And that is how I beat the
Baringhup Express. And Sir George was greatly
pleased with the whole thing, and ostentatiously
put a copy of the. Maryborough Advertiser—
printed on white silk for the occasion—in his
pocket. Before getting to Melbourne, no doubt,
he used it as a pocket-handkerchief, and blew his
vice-regal nose with it.
SILENUS.
Women who do not fancy work “do”
fancy work.
In England, milk passes from the dairy
farmer in the month of May at 4id. a gallon to
the consumer at 20d. a gallon : there is a margin
of £20,000,000 between the sum which the English
cowkeepers obtain and that which is paid by the
consumers of milk.
Sir Alfred Stephen asserts that Gaptah
Cook named two openings on the N.S.W. coast
which he failed to explore, after the joint Ad
miralty secretaries of his day, Jackson and
Stephens, as he had previously named two N.Z.
headlands. Sir George Jackson, when M.P. for
Weymouth and Judge Advocate of the fleet,
changed his name to Duckett, but the monumental
tablet to his memory at Bishop-Stortford, in
Hertfordshire, records that the names of Port
Jackson (N.S.W.) and Point Jackson (N.Z.) are
records of Captain Cook’s friendship for the de-
ceased baronet, on whose Yorkshire estate indeed
the marine explorer was born. Ihe myth as to
the naming of the port after the sailor on board
the Endeavour, who discovered it, is exploded by
the statement of Childers as First Lord of the
Admiralty that no sailor named Jackson was on
board that vessel.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, July 23,1890.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
Every woman whose chief delight is to sit at an angle of
80 degrees in the direction of her Gussie and let her tawny
looks tickle his jawbone in a sentimental way at dreamy
Intervals, will vote “Doll’s House” perfectly, sweetly
lifelike and go home fully convinced that it they weren’t
sure the cheap sentiment displayed by their Gussy wasn’t
purely spiritual, they would likewise leave him For Ever.
But the woman who reflects will probably smile cynically
—not at the high-flown “miracle of miracles,” but at the
idea of any caramel-chewing petted female leaving a
hubby, three pledges, and a comfortable home simply
because the old man, while in a state of semi-razzle-dazzle,
didn’t toe the spiritual scratch at a serious crisis ! But
“ Doll’s House ” shows femininity up in a perfectly charm-
ing light, and women hustle in the front seats with
exalted expressions in their eyes and smile in a
far-off tooth-achey way when addressed by degraded man,
who prefers his Nora Helmer d la first act, I know.
On 15th inst. Lord and Lady Carington were present and
were simply absorbed in the play. Lady Carington wore
a picturesque gown of wine-colour brocade, with elbow
Sleeves and full front of white gauze, white gloves,
diamond aigrette and bangles and Watteau fan, a lovely
managerial bouquet of unmanagerial appearance resting
before her ; Miss Walenn (who returns to England with
Lady 0.) pale yellow gauze relieved with brown, while
Captain Trench mounted guard with canary facings and
folded arms. There were lots of nice gowns in the circle.
Miss Hoffnuug wore yellow silk ; Miss Thorne, black .
Mrs. Kendall, white silk. Miss Aohuroh, who so superbly
plays Nora Helmer, w'ears Norwegian costume, the
clectrio-blue cloth gown edged with fur of the first act
being a dressmaker’s marvel—as is the terracotta and
brown fur frock worn during the next soulful rise of the
curtain.
Miss Achurch has caught on in Sydney as a woman’s
woman, and her dressing-room is thronged after the show
with emotional females in plush wraps who peck at her
frantically and gush in a weird, wild manner generally.
Your Sappho swelled the ranks the other evening and
found Miss Achurch in a pink and green floral dressing
jacket with a pound of vaseline on her face and hands.
“Don’t kiss me," she said, “until I have dried my
cheeks.” But the invading host pounced as usual. I
bate to see one woman kiss another. Such kisses are
matter in the wrong place—iust like clouds under the
finger-nails.
The only topic of conversation in Sydney at present is
the inwardness of Ibsen’s “ Doll’s House,” the comments
made on which strike me as very one-sided indeed. Everyone
appears to look at the matter from a she-standpoint; the
question invariably put being, “Is a woman justified in
leaving her husband when spiritual union is impossible?”
and nobody appears on the side of the defence individual-
ised in Torvald Helmer. Woman is generally conceded to
be hypersentimental and not so material as man, and
might (I don’t say would, for Woman as a rule is less able
to face the world alone than is Man) in a moment of poetic
exaltation, and with an idiot’s disregard of the after-effect
of her impulse, suddenly leave a husband who stood re-
vealed to her as a being of coarser instincts than she bar-
gained for; but how about the dozens of moderately
good men who have suddenly discovered that the woman
whom they married under the fixed impression that she
was very little short of an angel only melted willingly
into their arms from some ulterior motive, love of money,
luxury, a home—dozens of things? It is the very nature
of things under modern conditions that woman’s “ love ’»
should be far more mercenary than man’s- just look round
among your acquaintances and tell me if it is not so. I
a woman can leavea man because he isn’t the white exalt(s she
would make him,andwhich he didn’t wish toappear,surely
an honest man is at least equally justified in quitting the
society of a woman who stands revealed to him, both in
mind and in body, as a mere mass of pads. Now that I
have got almost to the end of this, I am cruelly told that
I have been unconsciously furnishing reasons for the
crusade of the Ibsenites against the prevailing state of
things.
After all, though one person deserting another because
his eye wasn’t lit up with the same fervent gleam as her own,
and he didn’t glare into the Beyond at the same aesthetic
angle, may he ideal ideality it’s not common sense. There
is no condition that has not its disadvantages and draw-
backs, and a woman wandering away from her husband,
for purely poetical objections, would very soon be sorry
she spoke. Men are not perfect—bless ’em !—but when she
found she had nobody to shy plates at or wither with
blighting sarcasm, or badger for a new bonnet, she would
want to lose her soul in a windy place and go back and
apologise. It is fashionable just now to howl matrimony
down, and one really conics to regard a matron as a
martyr in the tide of present popular opinion—but when
•me meets a single blossom on a withering stem and
listens to the drone of its discontented peevishness, one re-
cognises that it’s not only the marriage state that wantg
reviving, anyhow.
Save and except a reception Lady Carington held during
the early part of her reign, there never was such a one-
horse proceeding as the V.R. reception on 17th inst. Only
n smattering of people showed up and faded into the wet
again. The rain pattered dismally on the chairs placed
in the garden, and the Baron, in a blue tie, tried not to
look more tired than usual. Lady Carington, who flitted
from one damp person to another and made them forget
that their feet were wet, wore a home-gown of grey-green
woollen made with a plain skirt edged in the front with a
band of black lace, an artistic bodice with Zouave of black
lace and black lace sleeves, tan gloves and gold and
diamond bangles, and her hair beautifully dressed. Miss
Walenn wore grcen-aud-bluo tartan, wide dark-blue felt
hat and feathers; Mayoress Burdekin, heliotrope poplin
with velvet panels, Zouave of black lace and purple velvet
sleeves, bonnet in heliotrope, black lace and jet. Then
Sir John Robertson shook his mane on the scene ; a thin
bridegroom looked wretched as usual; and a gentleman
in howling gi;ey checks carried under his right arm a
paper parcel the size of six pairs of boots in a most charm-
ingly ddgagiS manner. The principal show of the after-
noon was the beautiful address painted by Mrs. Hunt and
presented to Lord and Lady Carington at Ryde, to com-
memorate “Arbor Day” on 16th inst.
Apropos to addresses, Lord and Lady Carington have,
through that dismal medium, achieved paintings of every
Australian flower, bird and butterfly. All the addresses
will be bound in a large book—even the unilluminated
foolscap outpourings are to be vice-regally cherished—on
the ptineiple, I suppose, that actuated the historical
prisoner who, on release, begged for his gaol cup and
platter “just to keep him from forgetting how miserable
he had been.”
The very latest gift from the engaged girl to her lover
is an embroidered pair of braces with their combined
monograms worked in her hair. By-the-by, gold-washed
girls are warned to leave these offerings strictly alone, as the
gilt comes off the hair even in the working, and if Adolphus
were confronted with a wishy washy monogram in mouse-
colour, instead of tawny gold, he would suspect aureoline
and fly to return no more.
Rain, sleet, wind and mud, with darts of sunshine just
to show how bedraggled you were, combined to make
Inaugural Arbor Day at Ryde on 16th inst.—a turned-up-
at-the-hems sort of ceremony, overshadowed by muddy
boots. The school was beautifully decorated with bunting
and blossoms, and the excellent luncheon was preceded
by an exhibition of work, and a concert given by the
pupils of the school, after which the tree-planting cere-
mony began. Owing to the descending torrents the leafy
formalities were brief. Lord Carington—who kept his
cuffs remarkably crisp considering everything—seized a
spade and buried the first root. Lady Carington (who
arrived after luncheon) followed suit, with an anxious eye
on the weather ; Mrs. Carruthers then grasped the garden
implement, scuffled a sapling into a large amateur hole
and retired hastily to shelter; and then almost everyone
stuck something in the boggy soil and filed in out of the
wet, and the proceedings terminated. Owing to the
awful weather no very special gowns resulted. Lady
Carington wore a slick navy-blue serge skirt, with Louis
front of cream chiffon with a diamond scarf-pin, fawn
covert coat, and bonnet of gold straw with cluster of pink
roses; Mrs. Carruthers (in mourning for her sister), a
black cashmere gown with flowing draperies, deepi crape
collar and cuffs, “Paul Jones” hat adorned with black
crape ; Mrs. Terry, drab silk, with bonnet en suite ; Mrs-
Darvall, black silk. On 25th inst., Lord Carington will
plant the first tree for the Fort-street School.
A number of Sydney suburban medicos have united in
B ; rcularising all the Parramatta friendly societies to the
effect that they will not meet in consultation the medical
officers of those institutions until the latter’s annual fees
are raised from their present rate of ten shillings pe
member; in a word, every medico selling his services at
that low figure will be boycotted by the Association. This
strikes me as a noble effort to maintain the prestige of the
science of medicine ; but with qualified doctors in every
rookery in every side street something had to be done, I
suppose. However, paper quarrels over the amount of
the fee aren’t quite in accordance with medical dignity,
and serve to bring the profession down a long peg.
Every doctor has a “ guinea muscle,” it is true, but, for
all that, he isn’t supppsed to know there is such a th ng as
a guinea, and the patient leaves it on the mantelpiece—
under the mat—anywhere, so long as Sawbones isn’t look-
ing, and he takes care he never is. The same, I afn told,
with the hill of costs, which barristers render to the
solicitor. The barrister does not add it up—professional
etiquette forbids—but he knows to ninepence how much
he ought to get, and he sees he gets it, too.
Which reminds me that lodge-doctors don’t, as a rule,
worry about their ten-or-twenty-shillings-a-year patients
A friend was dining with one of these doctors, and, ag
Doctor Forceps slashed into the roast-mutton, a ring was
hea.-d at the door, which Mrs. Forceps answered. “ Who
was it, my dear?” he asked. “Oh, only one of those
clubbers,” she said, with a sneer on her pretty little face;
“I told him you were engaged. They can’t expect to
monopolise you for the paltry fee. Won’t you have a
little celery sauce, Miss Johnson ?” And then the vegetables
were handed round.
Never have the “Knight and his Lady” pawed the
ivories and scattered the rosin to such advantage as at
their return concert on 19th inst. at the Centenary Hall,
when Lord and Lady Carington, with their little daughters
and. Mr. Seth Smith, and attended by Captain Trench,
were present, and the house was very full, notwithstand-
ing the soupy streets and curl-desuoying drizzle outside.
Lady Carington wore a black silk and lace gown with lace
elbow-sleeves, Medici collar and neck cut square frontally,
relieved with clusters of pale-pink roses and ferns, dia-
mond bracelets, and aigrette and long white gloves; the
other lady in the vice-regal party also wearing black,
while the little Caringtons were frocked in white silk and
blue. Lady Halle donned a plain gown in ruby velvet
with semi-detached train and a peppering of diamond
brooches on the corsage, shoes en suite. Mrs. Turner,
black lace and jet with pale-pink plume fan ; Miss Naylor
(who accompanied the Turners as usual) dawn-pink silk
with panels and folds of Maltese lace. Then here and
there in the high-backed pews were Carrie Swain in a pale
pink brocade and lace opera wrap with Medici collar over
a pale-blue silk gown and diamonds ; Frances Saville in
cream with bands of emerald green velvet, and a
large assortment of other fair notabilities. Sir John Hay
brought a large party of ladies, one wearing pale-pink
silk and a pink plume fan, another white silk beautifully
craped. A Margueritlsh get-up was in pumpkin-grey silk
cut squafe and filled in with pleated cream chiffon, of
which the puffed sleeves were made, Medici, collar and
reversof ruby velvet, grey gloves. Weber’s “Invitation
to the Dance,” played by Sir Charles, and the “ Barcarole’'
and “ Sherzo ” of Spohr rendered by Neruda, were the
encore pieces of the evening.
The reappearance of Miss Maggie Moore at Her
Majesty’s on 19th instant was greeted by a packed
popular house, which seemed to keenly appreciate the
little troubles of Meg, the pathetic if substantial waif in
verv short black clothes and very white.petticoats. There
were more dress-suits than evening bodices in the circle.
Clara Thompson wore a handsome red moire cloak with
bronze passementerie; and a large lady in black wore a
colossal diamond on the outside of soiled gloves. A lady
was present who dressed her hair like a Hindoo. In the
last act Alice Norton as Mrs. Radcliffe wore a Japanesy
gown in maize satin and crimson design ; Maribel Green-
wood, white silk, with electric-blue gloves and skirtal
ruche; and Bessie Colville sunflower-yellow silk with
bands of white silk and cluster of scarlet flowers. At the
end of the third act “Our Maggie” was loaded with
flowers.
One of the most interesting sights at high-class concerts'
that arise at long intervals in our midst, is the woman who
waves to and fro like a cobra-snake in time to the rhythm
of some classic “morso.” Vaises lend themselves readily
to their oscillation—one, two, three, backwards and for*
wards with an expression of sleepy soulfulness and your
eyes shut, but the time to observe the animated baton is
whten the piano-teaser is engaged on a selection in erratic
time. Then, if still at the game she resembles a reed
shaken by the wind, and the eccentricity of her orbit
causes her near neighbours to want their money back,
but if she is a hardened offender she keeps up a steady
sway while the pianist gyrates in nine-twelve time and
gets calmly out of beat with a confident smile as she
would say, “ Never mind—he will soon got the run of those
tenths.” I suppose they do it for amusement ?
The very latest idea, from a Sydney daily:—
TfiOß phrenologically-selected servants apply at Pro-
-* fessor ’s F.L. Agency.
Which opens quite a vista, doesn’t it ? Of course it seems
a very wise precaution to have Bridget's head prospected
before trusting her with the eternal welfare of your
infant or your own digestion-but, then, how far can phre-
nology be relied on ? She may have taken a header down-
stairs at her last place in a state of shocking intoxication
and so raised a bump indicating the most rigid teetotal
principles possible. Or she may have achieved a dint in
her skull through a key-hole having rushed suddenly
against her, said dint signifying an almost fastidious
objection to eaves-dropping on the part of the possessor.
And then, to reverse the picture, if the bump-scratoher
found an exceptionally wicked undulation on Bridget’s
cranium, all she has to do is to attribute it to a fall, or a
fight, or a bad cold, mark all the dark hints in her cranial
chart “ result of accident,” and come up like a lark at the
same old pound a week. I’m afraid this “ phrenologically-
selected servant ” won’t prove a very big advance on the
unprospected O’Hooligan, after all.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Sydney Reunions meet again at West’s, on Ist August.
The little Carington children don’t want to leave
Sydney, because, they say, “we have 82 real friends in
Sydney, and we don’t know our English cousins.” They
have quite forgotten their august relatives over the pond,
which isn’t surprising, as the eldest was but five when her
parents exiled themselves.
Lady Carington is looking extremely well just now.
From which it is inferred that her ladyship very seldom
reads the evening papers.

dance.
The American girl styles the divided skirt “ bifs,” which
is a contraction of bifurcated garments.
In Paris they call the new style of ladies’ riding horse-
back, man fashion, “La Oalifornichienne.” The Journal
Amusant depicts one of them at the Grand Prix races.
The marriage of Miss Edith Denne with Mr. Frederick
Campbell, of Duntroon, happened at St. John’s Church,
Darlinghurst, on 17th inst. The bride was attended by
four bridesmaids in white silk frocks and sovereign-yellow
sashes, hats en suite, and posies of buttercups and ferns
tied with yellow ribbons, while the bride wore the orthodox
chalky garments in brocaded satin with a court train,
Medici collar filled with lace, and superb bouquet of lovely
white orchids and ferns. The bridesmaids were Miss
Emily Denne, Miss Emily Street, Miss Marian Campbell,
and Miss L. Darley. Mrs. Denne held a reception at her
residence, “ Baroda,” Macleay-st., after the ceremony.
A very pretty wedding happened at St. James’ Church
on 17th inst. between Miss Graham, of Glebe Point, and
Mr. Edward Garland, of Bayswater-road, Darlinghurst.
The bridal costume on this occasion was very simple and
becoming—just , a soft white silk trained gown, with a
necklet of valuable pearls. Five bridesmaids saw her
through in 'the fashionable combination of cream and
yellow, with buttercup posies, and the wedding-party was
a haridsomelj’-gotten-up aggregate. Mrs. Ho ugh ton" wore

St. Andrew’s Cathedral was almost filled with spectators
to witness the marriage of Miss Ethel Terry and Mr.
Arthur Forrest, of Sydney, on 16th inst. The church was
beautifully decorated with white flowers and ferns and
palms, and the bride formed a pretty picture as she came
up with her four little maids in white muslin and moire
silk made very plainly with a foot ruche of lilies of the
valley and snowdrops. After the ceremony Mrs. Forrest
received the congratulations of her friends at Need’s
rooms.

The Private Cinderella, engineered by Messrs. A. A-
Smith and G. Callman, at West’s Rooms at 15th inst. was
a most enjoyable hop, though, owing to the bad weather,
several people omitted to be present. The dances were
varied by songs, Mr. Smith warbling “Only once more”
in beautiful voice, Mr. Fielding “Mona,” and Mr. Vincent
West gave “ For ever and for ever ” with great expression.
Of the gowns, Mrs. E. Shaw wore a handsome black silk
and lace, with clusters of saffron roses and diamond pins
in the hair; Miss Shaw, white silk and gauze and black
velvet bows; Miss Smith, white silk and pearls: Miss
McMahon, pink silk, Maltese lace, ard trails of blush roses.
Floor and supper were miles beyond comment.
The dance given by Mr. and Mrs. J. S. Carroll, of
“ Lugar Brae,” Waverley, on 16th inst., was somewhat
spoilt by the awful downpour, but about twenty-five
couples were present and enjoyed themselves immensely.
Mrs. Carroll wore black silk and lace ; Miss Carroll, white
satin and white jet; Miss Mabel Carroll, cream lace ; Miss
Haddocks, pale-blue silk and gauze; Miss Button, gobelin-
blue, with white scarves ; a handsome gown was in cream-
silk and Russian net, with crimson feather-fan. The
supper-table was beautifully decorated with the invariable
fairy lamps.

A Milanese lady left her body to the town
to cremate, and requested that her ashes might be
exhibited in a glass urn in order to show the
public what the result of cremation really is. The
glass urn, containing a little heap of pearl-white
ash, stands at present on a marble pedestal near
the chapel in the great cemetery of Milan, and in
front of it is the photograph of the lady taken
during her lifetime. In London, the other day,
there was exhumed the body of a woman buried in
the year 1789. The corpse, which was that of a
finely-developed woman, bore but slight evidence
(says the British Medical Journal) of having
lain in the grave for a hundred years. So far
from showing signs of returning to its native dust,
it appeared to have turned into a dirty white wax.
It was almost entirely converted into adipocere—
skin, fat, muscles, and bones ; indeed with the ex-
ception of the leg bones and hands, the whole
material of the frame was turned, into this sub-
stance. The features were perfectly distinguish-
able, and any person who had known deceased in
life would have readily identified her. The hair
was partly covered by a closely-fitting cap; the
teeth were all in place.

“Meg, or the Castaway” is an atrocity
whose perpetrator trusts to evade justice by keep-
ing his name out of the day-bills. This neuralgia
in four acts is somewhat alleviated by the pre-
sence of Maggie Moore, who drags sweet melodies
into inane company for no other reason except
their saccharinity, and jigs on the carefully-sanded
wood-blocks of the Borough with the grace and
ankles that captured our susceptible heart 1G
years ago. This is the story;—Maggie Moore,
largely engaged in the match-industry, rescues
Violet Aston, alias Baby Nicholls, late of the
“Doll’s House,” from a savage bull-dog, who is
thereupon quietened by a blow from a shovel.
Frank Harcourt, in the dog-trade, presents his
little account, “to dead dog, £30,” and is
biffed off the premises at once by Harry Jewett
—virtuous for this occasion only—but returns
at a later hour and carries off the twenty-pound
child in order thattheoffice of family scrap-devourer
in his household may not remain vacant. The
unfeeling parents, Alice Norton and Hazlitt, don’t
seem to bother much over the loss, but at once
skip to the.Continong, presumably for a change of
heir. Jewett, however, at once sacrifices his
beauty, arid disguises himself as a coffee-stall
keeper, who searches for the lost kinchin with two
voices, one badly cracked, some broken china, a
tin of prepared coffee, and half-a-pound of the best
moist. He drops across Meg, whom anxiety has
kept from growing during an interval of two years
between acts, and they rescue Violet Aston, whose
growth has also been suspended, from the kid
and dog-napping Harcourt, who, by running him
in the water-cress profession, is rapidly amassing
a fortune and many grog-blossoms. Then Jewett,
who inadvertently has swallowed some of his own
coffee, falls sick and is nursed by Meg, who is
finally arrested because she didn’t steal a purse from
Harry Hoyt, a flaxen-haired idiot who lugs about
with him another awful imbecile named Lloyd.
After she has “done her stretch ” Meg is released
in time to see the restoration of the suit of
knickerbockers—on whom she lavishes an affection
fit for a Windsor uniform—to the arms of its
parients, who have not filled the vacancy in their
aching bosoms during its absence. Meg’s devotion
is finally rewarded by the hand of the coffee-stall
philosopher, now returned to decent society,
whose dress-suit presents a wider field for the
operations of her unbounded love than the little
“Lord Fauntleroy ” around wtiioh it has hitherto
enfolded itself. In describing this play we have
been obliged (having mislaid the programme) to
use the names of the actors instead of the charac-
ters they pourtray ; this will not prevent us saying
that Maggie Moore is every bit as blithe, tuneful,
active, and pathetic as of yore, and that Harry
Jewett, in a very lengthy and dual part, is
effective. The chance may be valuable to him in
the future ; it has shown what The Bulletin pre-
viously prognosticated—that “character” is likely
to be his strong point. The other folks have little
to do but stand round and look silly, and it is a
pity to see good people saddled with such impos-
sible and unutterable tommyrot.

80 far the “ Doll’s House ” controversy
has failed to occupy the only truly debateable
ground. Even Ibsen’s warmest admirers admit
that it is not a play in the conventional sense of
the term; therefore, here is no room for argu-
ment, and why there should be any as to whether
the conduct of Nora is reprehensible or otherwise,
is not evident in a community that swallows a
Lady Macbeth, a Camille, a Floria Tosca, and
countless meaner and more vicious creations with-
out murmuring. The real point for consideration
is as to whether Nora represents a type of woman-
hood, not whether the lesson conveyed by her
conduct is of good or bad import. In Torvald,
Mr. Titheradgc has a very difficult character
to feel, and therefore to impersonate--the actor
says the most difficult part of his life. If his per-
formance has a failing, it is that he does not, to
our mind, sufficiently accentuate the dismay and
astonishment that must overtake the martinet
confronted by the revolt of one who has,
apparently, heretofore been as clay in his hands.
It is usually assumed that it would be utterly im-
possible for Helmer, the hide-bound prig, to
emancipate himself from the fetters which custom
and nature have riveted around his soul, but if
suffering had power to awaken the dormant spirit
of the once frivolous Nora, it is not evident that
the deeply-moved man—engaged when the cm’tain
falls in thinking out his position—will not also
achieve spiritual deliverance. Krogstadt is a
stronger and fuller nature, thrust by circumstance
into tortuous - paths, desirous of better things and
capable of their attainment. Easier to imper-
sonate than either of the leading parts because it
is pitched throughout in one key, it demands from
its impersonator a faculty for the simulation of
intense feeling held in check by an iron will.
These conditions are admirably fulfilled by Her-
bert Flemming. None but a finished and
thoroughly-capable actress could carry Mrs. Lin-
den through several scenes almost devoid of action.
Perhaps Miss Enson is weakest at the moment
when her heart goes out to the lover of her youth.
Quietly played, the Dr. Rank of Charles Charring-
ton is effective, but in one exit he strains to simu-
late bodily weakness in a manner for which the
story furnishes no justification. By the way, “ A
Doll’s House” —best known to English-reading
communities through the medium of Miss Lord’s
translation, entitled “Nora” —was unsuccessfully
introduced to the British stage over five years ago
by Jones and Herman’s adaptation, styled
“ Breaking a Butterfly.” “ Frou-Frou ” succeeds
this play, which, so far, has had a most successful
run, on Saturday night.
