The Reverend Charles Clark, whose re-
semblance to a dignified and glorified cherub
increases with the lapse of years, has been doing
good business at Sydney School of Arts. So far,
the most successful recitals (as a draw) have been
from the writings of the kindly and kind-loving
Dickens, who in Mr Clark has a sympathetic in
terpreter, one animated by a similar hmnani
tariamsm, and whose own jokes are evolved with
that elaborate attention to detail which charac
torised the great humourist. The last entertain
ments are announced—“ Macaulay ” for Thurs-
day, and “ Thackeray ’’ for Friday evening.

The Halles’ second Sydney season has
been most successful, and Norman-Neruda is such
a tremendous favourite that she has only to poke
|,er head out of the retiring-room to get an ovation.
When Baron Carington picked my Lady up there
the otner evening after the interval—-he had been
presiding with Rupert at a Mothers’ Meeting at
the Y.M.C.A. dear, dear, what a lark that
was!-—the quick eye of the lady - violinist
spotted them as soon as she came on again, and
she gave two saucy, little nods, as if to say—
“ How-do, boys; glad you’ve come; better late
than never, I guess.” The Halles are going some-
where, probably to New Zealand, shortly.

We read that “Considerable amusement,
not unmixed with indignation, is felt here
(Bourke, N.S.W.) at the fiasco in Sydney Theatre
Royal on Saturday week on the occasion of the
presentation to Miss Nellie Stewart of a wreath
of flowers nominally in the name of the women of
Bourke. Whilst all here fully appreciate Miss
Stewart’s generosity, still other more pressing
affairs claimed their attention. The wreath pre-
sented was never known or thought of in Bourke.”
When women present flowers to anybody, you may
safely bet that she is a queen, or a duchess, or a
countess —a “Lady” at the very least. Men
rarely associate to present flowers to anybody who
hasn’t a symmetrical arm, a well-developed bust
and nice ankles. The much-mashed givers of our
Nellie's posy were far too bashful to make the
presentation in their own names, so they sought bp
fasten the responsibility on to the women.
#* * *
At Melbourne Bijou, on Saturday night,
there was a crammed house to welcome a revival
of “ The Pickpocket, ” a well-balanced shriek which
has apparently been gaining in popularity during
the last three and a half years. Of the original
B. and B. cast only two members remain in their
old places ; but as one of these is the all-important
waiter, and as the changes are an improvement
when judged in the lump, “ The Pickpocket”
should be putting in his allotted six nights to
fast and furious business. A company including
Boueicault, Anson, Cecil Ward, Lyndal, Emma
Temple, Pattie Browne, and Miss Romer cannot
fail to bring out the bright points of a farcical
comedy when there are any such to bring out.
“The Pickpocket” is resplendent with them;
wherefore the necessity for next Saturday’s change
to “Turned Up,” a comparative chestnut, is not
strikingly apparent. Having the comedy game
in their own hands just at present, Messrs. B. and
B. seem to be playing for safety overmuch. How-
ever, the fault is a good one —for the public.
-*■ * *
Other Melbourne theatres are renewing
their old bills. A racing season following heavy
rains has improved the returns at some shows,
notably in the case of “ Romeo and Juliet. ” At
the Alexandra, too, a first-class performance with
the additional inducement of popular prices,
appeal both to the intelligence and the trousers-
pocket, like a clearing sale at enormous reductions.
“Master and Man” thrives accordingly, and the
relations between Bland Holt and his dog, Beelze-
bub, are of the most cordial character.
* * *
Old Jack Saxton continues to hump
“Now-a-Days” along at the Opera House with
strength and energy worthy of a better cause.
The management protest that “audiences have
steadily increased ” since the opening night, while
a section of the daily press discovers that the com-
pany supporting William Rignokl has much im-
proved ; which announcements are rather at
variance with previous ones to the effect that the
house was always overflowing and the cast unsur-
passable throughout. No matter. The Royal
pines and droops under that gloomy, explosive
calamity, “The Black Country,” yet several of
the parts are played more than well. Clyndes has
been coming out in his best style—strong, broad, a
little monotonous, but not lacking in bits of deli-
cate finish, withal. In the sensation scene he
throws off a well-remembered howl of despair,
the howl that used to fetch ’em at the
Grecian in days of yore, before that tem-
ple of blood-and-thunder drama and George
Conquestian pantomime was bought up by
General Booth for Hallelujah purposes. O,
what a fall was there ! Edith 'Blande, Maude
Williamson, and Royco put some pleasing work
into “The Black Country,” but their genius is
obscured by the idiotio behaviour of an object in
an Eton jacket, of the venerable driveller, who
desires to silence everybody, and the long, heavy
villain who is fated to dodge behind the corner
and overhear everybody’s conversation, after
which he stalks forward exclaiming, “Ha !” or
“So !” until he becomes nauseating. There never
was such a drama for dodging round the corner,
or glaring through the keyhole.
* * #
Next Saturday, however, the Royal should
make a fresh spurt with “Formosa,” that same
notable piece of impossibility not having been seen
in Melbourne for 20 years, they say. “Formosa ”
is magnificently naughty, and either of the tall
goddesses—Williamson or Blando —is fitted for
the name-part.
** * *
Melbourne Gaiety (under the Bijou) was
re-opened on Saturday by Slade Murray, who has
worked up a mixed entertainment, terminating in
a burlesque founded on the Sydney pantomime
‘‘Aladdin.” A “first part” is honourably dis-
tinguished by the absence of the gruesome inter-
locutor, who usually sits in the middle and incites
a couple of remorseless fiends to pelt him with
conundrums. Some varieties are unduly drawn
out, but the burlesque, supplying the eternal
demand for legs, comes like a boon and a blessing
to men whose appetite for the article is not too
fastidious. The Phonograph and a few other
shows are holding on to all they can get.

that the heneficiare's connection with the
aforesaid bulky periodical was severed in con-
sequence of his inclination, on a certain
memorable occasion, to bestow jam where the
Argus had not even smeared butter, consequently
the action of the purfesh is highly proper and
quite characteristic of their sweet tooth. More-
over, any movement for supplying any dramatic
critic with a lump sum is good, very good. The
Bulletin respectfully recommends to the notice of
managers, actors, and musicians, the claims of
many “critics” of quite another calibre still
on the job, men and women who have
grovelled at their feet in the past, and,
which is of more importance, will grease their
boots in the future, as long as life holds out. Too
long has the Press gushed along without getting a
benefit matinee, the proceeds to be divided accord-
ing to number of eulogistic adjectives published
per annum. Perhaps critics themselves are chiefly
to blame for this. So long as yards of drivel can
be bought with a casual drink, a bad cigar, or a
managerial smile, the reptile press (which wo take
to mean critics who crawl) will never earn a meet
reward in hard cash.
# # 9
But all this must not be taken as a drive at Dr.
James Edward Xeild, charitable physician and
cultured man of letters, on whom fortune has not
smiled of late, and who has made sacrifices for the
upholding of his personal independence. The
Bulletin is not unwilling to believe that the
theatrical profession really and truly admires and
is from the bottom of its heart grateful to cheery
old Doctor Neild —than whom no more interesting
man ever trod Australian soil —for the inestimable
services he has rendered to the drama and to the
poor players whom he has ever made welcome at
his hospitable home.
*» * *
The Sydney Morning Herald is alto-
gether out in comparing the reception given to
“A Doll’s House,” at Sydney Criterion, with
the patient hearing accorded to Ibsenism at Mel-
bourne Princess’s. In Sydney the first act was
recklessly guyed, and a reaction necessarily set in.
At the Princess’s the first act was received with
perfect silence, the seoond with an occasional
titter, and the third with ridicule at its close.
When Miss Achurch said “ Good-bye, Torvald,” in
sepulchral tones, the gallery boys wished him
good-bye likewise. It was a pardonable way of
intimating that they wanted him to clear. Again,
when Nora said “ Does nothing strike you ?” the
whole house roared inwardly. People evidently
thought that the husband —then played by Mr.
Charrington—should be struck by something. But
there was no real guying of the show, other than
in the vehement applauding of Herbert Flemming
whenever he came on to make matters fairly lively.

“ The Private Secretary” has once more
taken a strong hold on the Adelaide public, and
the Rev. Robert Spalding—“that miserable
worm —throws his emaciated form around to the
delight of large audiences. Frank Thornton, it is
to be regretted, was very unwell, and for two or
three nights it was with great difficulty he ap-
peared. Ho has now recovered, however. Har-
wood re-appears as Cattermole and has plenty of
fresh “gags,” and Eille Norwood warbles.with
success, while Frank Cates, Charlie Brown, Ned
Corlesse, Annie Taylor, Ada Leo, Brenda Gibson,
arid Emma Brontou assist in their own excellent
way. When business shows signs of drooping,
“ Sweet Lavender ” will be staged.

Adelaide, expressed his intention of returning to Aus-
tralia and taking Adelaide Theatre Royal. He has made

There is some talk of building a second theatre in
Adelaide.
S. Herai/d reports Rev. Charles Clark to have said, in
his lecture on “ The Tower of London,” “ There was not a
nation that could claim to be civilised that did not
cherish a most profound and permanent reverence for the
battle-grounds of its liberties, the sepulchres of its
monarchs, and the temples of its God.” Now, Charles
Clark surely did not say anything at all about the
“sepulchres of its monarchs.” He said—paraphrasing
Macaulay in a “ La}' of Ancient Rome”—“the sepulchres
of its fathers, and the temples of its gods,” when we
heard him about fourteen years ago, and we presume that
even the Tory atmosphere of England has not made him
substitute king for father.

£‘545- and this is a question that involves, incidentally,

during the past eight years, in fact, it involves the con-

David Christie Murray has given the original MS. of
“Chums” to Sir George Grey, who said when accepting
it —“ I shall present this to the Auckland Library. lam
not flattering you when I say that it is very valuable, and
would he so even if it were the poorest thing ever written,
for it is the first thing ever done by an English man of
letters for New Zealand.” Sir George also said that in
“ Chums” he had “for the first time seen human nature
staged unstagily.”
David Christie Murray says he could not have had a
20 per cent, better representation of “Chums” in London
than that given by _St. Maur’s company. He intends to
write a series of dramas on an Australasian foundation.

is “Mates.”

It seems as if Mary Anderson’s assertion that she has
finally abandoned the stage hears the stamp of truthful-
ness. She has taken no farewell tours. The stage-career
of the performer who starts farewell-touring can never be
stayed by marriage ; in fact, by no catastrophe hut death.

if Leon turned up. He was familiar in Melbourne streets
a few months hack, and within the last three months he
visited The Bulletin office at Sydney.
Now we have an observer going to a church gallery,
and declaring he sees more bald heads down below than
in the stalls, wherefore piety causes baldness. Confound
it, we arc had every way. This ingenious writer imagines
a bald head making love, pietic or otherwise, to a girl,
and just on the point of success, when she moralises on
three flies crawling on the shiny billiard-ball.

The Premier Firmament Building
Association Prosecution.
A Melbourne Police Court Comedy.
The hearing of the charges of conspiracy against
the directors and others interested in the above-
mentioned joke was resumed at 10.30.
Mr. Panton, P.M., presided. The Bench of
honorary magistrates was not very full before the
luncheon adjournment, but subsequently it seemed
to become as full as might be expected.
Mr. Purves, Q.C., Mr. Cold ham, &c., held fat
briefs for the prosecution ; Sir Bryan O’Loghlen,
Mr. Gaunson, Mr. Theodore Fink, Mr. Lyle, and
others appeared for the rollicking rams out of the
dock.
The case having been called,
Mr. Gaunson wished to know whether the
Bench intended to hang Colonel Templeton,
without the option of a fine, before proceeding
further with this most monstrous charge. Time
was being wasted in hearing objectionable evi-
dence against the defendants, who were not used
to that sort of thing, and desired to leave the
Court, without a stain upon their characters, as
quickly as possible.
Mb. Fink.—Right you are, says Moses.
Sib Bkyan. — l beg to call the attention of the
Age reporter to the fact that Mr. Purves is picking
his teeth. Under the circumstances, I think it
would be more decent of my learned brother to
apologise to these influential parties (pointing to
the defendants) and ask the Lord to have mercy on
his soul.
Me. Pubves. —If I can’t pick my teeth I’ll pick
a quarrel.
Sib Bryan. —Another insult to ould Ireland.
Mb. Gaunson. - Can you pick a dead bird for
the Steeple? (Loud laughter, during which the
Age representative prepared heading for next morn-
ing’s report: “Mr. Gaunson makes short work of
the. Prosecution. ”)
Mr, Panton. —Let us get on with the hearing.
Much amusement was caused by the frolics of l
the defendants at this stage of the proceedings.
They were discovered to be playing “Buck,
buck,” Messrs. Doherty, the Corkhills, and Mur-
phy acting as bucks, while the more distinguished
defendants sat on their respective backs.
Mb. Panton.—Gentlemen, do, please, resume
your seats and stick to euchre.
Mb. Mi bams (to Doherty). —Buck, buck, how
many fingers do I hold up ?
Mb. Panton (sternly). —lf you don’t play a
quieter game I must certainly commit you.
Mb. Gaunson. —Well, why doesn’t the Crown
provide them with a football?
Mb. Lyle.—Or engage some nice girls to come
"and tickle tnem under the chin. The gentlemen
must put in their time somehow.
Mb. Purves. —They ought to devote their minds
to repentance.
Mb. Gaunson.— Repentance ! Ain’t it sick'n-
ing ? We haven’t come here to be bullied.
The Court's attention was attracted to the fact
that one of the Murphys had gone outside to see
a man, and the case was adjourned for an hour
until he came back.
Mb. Panton (sternly).- -Where have you been
this time, sir? Did you go out to have another
Turkish bath?
Missing Murphy.—O, I’ve been keeping dark
off the weather bow.
Mr. Panton. — If it occurs again you will be
chained up. Let us get on with the case.
Mb. Gaunson. —As I was saying, the insect
Purves is downright sick’ning.
Murphy (in a stage whisper). —Fight him for a
fiver, and I’ll hold the stakes.
Mr. Coldham. —Ah, you want some more loans,
I see.
Mb. Gaunson (excitedly).— None of your dirty
insinuations. Get on the lawyers’table, and I’ll
fight you for any stakes you like.
Mr. Panton.—We will all fight for steaks, with
onions. The court is adjourned for luncheon.
After an interval of two hours the Court re-
turned, looking much refreshed.
Mb. Bunge, a speculative builder, was examined
by Mr. Purves, and deposed that he remembered
the land boom before it went bung. Couldn’t say
for certain why it went bung, hut thought the
banks were responsible. Didn't believe in banks
himself, but always kept his money in a stocking.
Mb. Gaunson. —That won’t wash. ( Great ap-
plause, which was continued until Mr. O. stood, on
Mr. Lyle's head and bowed his acknowledgements.)
Mr. Bunge, resuming, said that he had gone
over the Elsternwick proper y, and valued it at
something between twopence and three millions
sterling. Some people would call it dear at two-
pence, while others considered it dirt cheap at the
larger figure. People varied in their ideas,
especially in building societies. Some of the
houses were finished, and he thought they would
be suitable residences for his mother-in-law.
Cross-examined by Mr. Gaunson, the witness
stated that he didn’t know whether the defendants
were whiter than snow, but supposed they would
be if whitewashed.
Mb. Gaunson.— Did you ever see any of the
defendants before to-day ? Remember you are on
your oath.
Witness. — Well, on my oath I can’t see any of
them now.
Mb. Panton (sternly). —What is the meaning of
this—have they all gone on the loose again ?
Mb. Lyle indignantly denied that defendants
had gone astray as suggested. They were playing
a quiet game of skittles in the lobby.
Mb. Panton. —l cannot allow this. Yesterday
afternoon they took a trip to Ballarat, and now
they are playing skittles.
Mb. Purves. —It's a-way they've got.
Mb. (r AUNSON. —None of your nasty innuendoes.
You deserve to be set up for a ninepin yourself.
Mb. Coldham.—For a pun-ish-mcant. (Load
laughter, during which one of the honorary magis-
trates rolled under the Bench and took no further
part in the proceedings),
Mr. Panton : Bring in the defendants.
Mil. Gaunson.— -They can’t come just now;
they have finished skittles and are tossing for
drinks with the Ministry.
Mr. Fink (rushing in excitedly)'.—Nebuchad-
nezzar ! here’s a- lark—Duncan is let ; in for the
liquors !
Mr_, Gaunson.—Serves him right for permitting
this ridiculous prosecution to continue. I don’t
know where he expects to go to.
Mr. Lyle. —l guess they are all going to .the
Blue Lion. Pretty bit of muslin at the private-
bar.
Mr. Purves.—Wait till we get them at the
public-bar. ,
Mr. Gaunson.— None of your disgusting insults,
you are nothing but a cowardly bully.
Mr. Coldham.—lf a cow, ’ardly a bull he.
(General collapse of counsel , who fell upon the floor
and roared laughter.)
Mr, Panton.— Let us get on with the case with-
out the defendants, I would certainly commit
them if they were present.
The Court Orderly. — l beg your pardon for
interrupting your worships, bub there are six wit-
nesses in the witness-room, beastly drunk. They
ordered a hogshead of beer and bathed in it.
Here’s one of them. Look at him, your worships,
he won’t put his clothes on.
Und raced Witness. —Three cheers for Build
Shassiety !
A constable here stated that he was quite worn-
out with rescuing the witness from drowning.
Mr. Panton : Who is this unclad person ? Do
you know anything of him, Mr. Lyle ?
Mr. Purves : He had been seen taking bags i f
lollies from the Murphys.
Mr. Lyle : That’s false.
Mr. Panton : I cannot allow such expressions
to be used.
Mr. Lyle : Then I will merely say that t! ®
learned O.C. is a liar of the deepest dye, and I
respectfully ask that the Crown shall not be
allowed to make any more witnesses drunk on the
premises. The Attorney-General amuses Irinself
by throwing sovereigns into the hogshead while
these poor fellows dive for them. The top of the
f
cask was staved in by the Crown Solicitor on
purpose. t
Mr. Purves. —Nonsense, you know very well
that it is all Nirarao’s doing. He pretends that
he wants to make “ shocking examples ” of them.
Mr. Panton (sternly). —-Orderly, take the wit-
ness into my private room and wash the beer off
him. Let us get on with the case.
The cross-examination of Bnnce by Mr. Gaunson
was then resumed.
Mr. Gaunson.—Are you aware, sir, that the
defendants are as innocent as Mary’s little lamb ?
Mr. Paxton. The character of Mary’s little
lamb is not in evidence.
Mr. Purves.— The Crown doesn’t object to the
question; the young sheep referred to was a
fleecy animal.
Mr. Gaunson.— l will not submit to your vil-
lainous interruptions. I move that this prepos-
terous charge against gentlemen of known integrity
be referred to a select Parliamentary committee.

sentients.)

non est man. (Laughter.)

A brewer, interviewed by a Sydney daily
paper, has the calmness to state that the strong
English beer imported to Australia in bulk would
not even at the same price hold its own in the
market against colonial beer. If that brewer will
send along his name, lie shall have a duplicate of
the champion belt by return post.
Here is an extract from some verses by
Lalmohun Ghose, a young man of purely Indian
parentage and English education ; —
Deep-shaded will I lie, and deeper yet
In night, where not a leaf its neighbour knows ;
Forget the shining of the stars, forget
The vernal visitation of the rose ;
And, far from all delights, prepare my heart’s repose.
O crav e not silence thou 1 too soon, too sure,
Shall autumn come, and through these branches wee));
Soon birds shall cease, and flowers no more endure ;
And thou beneath the mould unwilling creep,
And silent soon shall he in that eternal sleep.
Green still il is. where that fair goddess stravs,
Then follow, till around thee all he sete.
Lose not a vision of her passing face ;
Nor miss the sound of her soft robes, that here
Sweep o’er the wet leaves of the fast-falling veor
This is the man to the colour of whose skin Lord
Salisbury on. a memorable occasion somewhat con-
temptuously alluded.
The S.'A. mouse-yarn season is now over.
After careful consideration of the claims of the
competitors, The Bulletin has decided to award
the palm to the gentleman who wrote this :
The live mice were in such numbers that it was only
necessary to lift an empty sack, and a hundred or more
would be prancing about your feet. The dead mice are
past counting ; the ground is strewn with them, and in
many places there are thousands in a heap, so that the
smell is horrible. One day during the week 30 mice
entered a box at the public school whilst the children
were in school. The master shut the lid, and the children
killed them when school was out. Although the prevailing
colour is grey, yet some white ones have been captured,
and a nice roan-coloured one was caught last week.
Three boys, the eldest being 12, spent their last holiday in
catching mice, and they caught 6520. Several fanners
have been catching 6000 a week, and a friend of mine
caught 21 in a gin-bottle. It contained a little oil, and
they went after it until the bottle was full, and the top
one had been partly eaten by his comrades.
On application at this office the champion belt,
which has been held by one of our staff for five
years, will be handed over. The finishing spurt in
the above yarn defies competition.
The history of exploration is full of mean
yarns. For instance, it is related of one illustrious
Australian explorer that he used to secretly steal
sugar from the camp-supply for his own use, at a
time when his party were on short rations. The
complete story of the Burke and Wills affair
has not been told, but it may be one of
these days; and now Stanley, speaking at
tlm Savage Club (some of the members of
which hissed him at a recent dinner), has
painted. Emin Pasha as a weak-minded, slothful
sensualist. ‘ ‘Some trenchant examples were given of
the Pasha’s vacillation, naturally irritating to the
verge of desperation to a man of Stanley’s calibre,
and a story told of the mischievous effects of the
German’s fatal fondness for fair women. This
yarn showed how Emin’s chief officer discovered
that the Pasha had seduced his favourite
wife’s affection. The irate husband thereupon
swore to kill either Emin or himself or his wife,
before sunset. Emin fled to Stanley for protec-
tion, which the explorer granted, but only
on condition that he was permitted to arrange
the matter as he chose. He then had the lady
brought before him and insisted on her returning
to her husband. She said, ‘Never! never!
never!’ and, unveiling, exposed extraordinary
charms. But Stanley was adamant, and would
admit of no compromise. After three interviews,
at each of which the damsel repeated ‘ Never !
never ! never !’ she accepted Mr. Justice Stanley's
(?) flat, and crept away to her husband’s hut. He
met the lady in a right spirit, and they livid
happily ever after. ” Now let us hear Emin’s story.

Matt. Morgan, artist, has died in America.
He sprung into fame with, that slashing Toma-
hawk, which, 22 years ago, lashed the Prince of
Wales, especially with a cartoon of the ghost of
George IV. The thing was nobbled, and Matt,
turned to artistic work in the States, as cartoonist,
theatrical picture-poster man and scene-painter.
He missed a great career through the nobbling.
Queen Victoria has lost her oldest friend,
Lady Ely, sister-in-law of Lord Augustus Loftus
—which accounts for the milk in the eocoanut.
Her Majesty used to call Lady Ely “ Janey,” and
Janey didn’t forget Gussy. Victoria and Janey
were friends nigh 50 years, quite like Queen Anne
and the Duchess of Marlborough, with their Mrs.
Morley and Mrs. Freeman. “Vicky, give Gussy
some more pie.”
(Sir William Fraser says that Venables,
Q.C., was the breaker of Thackeray’s nose in that
tight at Charterhouse School. Let us disinter a
venerable story about a relative of Venables,
Q.C., aforesaid. Mr. Henry Venables, when
Secretary of the Victorian Education Department,
inadvertently addressed as “ Mr. Stewart ” a
gentleman who took considerable pride in the fact
that his name was “ Stuart.” The affronted scion
nf royalty insolently directed his reply to “Mr,
Vegetables, Secretary, Education Department.”
A Molong (N.S.W.) clergyman says he is
told that the Right Hon. W. E. Gladstone-one
of England’s greatest (if not the greatest) orators
and statesmen—has to be watched like a child-
thief ; he has gone to the hatter’s and toy-shops and
ordered 200 hats at once for himself, takes lease of
an expensively-furnished room, clears it out, sends
the furniture away to auction, then refits up the
room with dolls and tops as a baby-house. His
wife has had to follow him into shops and counter-
mand the silly orders of the almost nonagenarian
debtor, and ask tradesmen nut to execute such
orders and release him from responsibility incurred
till his whim is over.

i he Bulletin notices that a large per-
centage of that small number of people who
publicly change their religions are appearing on
the relig o-political platforms in Australia. When
a man (or woman) “ travels ” on the fact that he
has turned his back on the creed in which he was
born and ostentatiously reviles that creed, he is
affrays an impostor and devoid of true religious
feeling. There are good men of every religion and
every religion is good—from Christ’s down to Joe
Smith’s, to them who honestly believe in it. The
man who makes political capital out of his alleged
change of belief is despised by both sides.
The people of N.S. Wales, according to
official statistics, only consume 2.88 gallons per
head per annum of proof alcohol, of wine, spirits
and beer, while in Victoria, according to the State
returns, 4.10 gallons per head are required per
year. The people of N.S.W. are not more sober
than the Victorians, but they drink more “sly”
and doctored grog which pays no duty. Scores of
thousands of gallons of white spirit have been
secretly distilled in George-street, Sydney, under
the noses of the police, and one of the very biggest
fortunes made in Australia was founded on fire-
water manufactured on the premises. The enter-
prising gentleman is now dead, and his memory is
respected by all who never knew him thoroughly.

Dear Bulletin, —The parody on Volapiik
in your paper of to-day, purporting to be from
the opera “Plofed,” is amusing, and might be
taken by some to be the genuine article, though it
appears to be a mere jargon of pigeon English,
German, Prcnch, mediaeval Latin, and Volapiik.
It may interest you to know that, in spite of ridi-
cule and many obstacles, the intrinsic merit of
Volapiik is gaining for it official recognition.
Thus in Russia the Board of Censors, who
formerly absolutely prohibited Volapiik literature
because unintelligible to them, have learnt the
language and, being now able to read it, admit
such literature when in itself unobjectionable.
Again, a telegram transmitted from one part of
N.S.W. to another in the Volapiik language was
charged the extra rate for cipher messages. How-
ever, the head-office in Sydney decided to charge
the telegram at the same rate as telegrams
in English, French, German, &c.; and promptly
returned the extra charge. The department may
thus be said to have officially recognised Volapiik.
— Yours, &c., Arthur W. Bateman.— Manly,
19th July, 18'JO.

Society
As the Doll s House” is still a theme for
discussion among all who witness the drama, it is
well that people should know that R. S. Smythe
•suggests a very prosaic solution of the difficulty.
Inc author, Henrik Ibsen, is a rabid teetotaller,
f, I rl I niai 11 tains that his object in the
Doll s House ’ was to show what may happen to
a model wife and mother if she goes to a dance
where there are refreshments. In the last act,
a ter the ball, it is evident that both Torvald and
Hr. Rank have drunk too much champagne. The
Much-travelled, who has had a wide experience
the sex, contends that Mrs. Torvald had not
been a total abstainer that evening. This, he
■contends, is the most charitable explanation of
J> ora s delusion in fancying that, after being
married eight years and having three children,
she had been living with “a strange man,” and of
the heartless desertion of her family; and he has
no doubt that next morning, when in her sober
senses, Nora would return to her conjugal and
maternal duties. So that the fizzle with which
fa , )s House ” finishes is simply the result
of fizz ; but Torvald was too drunk to notice it.
According to a similar method of interpretation,
modern commentators have attributed the sudden
conversion of Saul of Tarsus to sunstroke.

t( The latest swindle on the board is the
‘‘ education contest.” Every newspaper contains
ads. worded thus:—“Literary contest.—£so
to the winner. Form two words from the letters
beapoup. ’ As many guesses as you like by
letter, N.B.—Send six stamps with each guess.
Competition closes on Ist August. Address
‘Abracadabra, Post-office, Smith-street.” Brown,’
only two removes from an idiot, sees this “ad ”
He’d like to earn £5O badly, and after staring at
the letters for an hour, it suddenly strikes him
with the force of a Nasymth hammer, that “ pea-
soup ” is the only possible solution of the problem.
Then he feels his skull to make sure that he hasn't
shattered his mighty intellect, begins to regret that
hedidn’t take to literary pursuits earlier in life, then
writes with a trembling hand “ pea-soup,” in large
letters on a sheet of foolscap, to which he attaches
six stamps, kisses his wife, hinting darkly at a
turn of luck, &c., and then rushes out to post his
application for the winning cheque. Smith, Jones
and Robinson, witli all the liquor-prohibitionists
and school girls and Y.M.C.A. members in town
do the same, and so many “ pea-soups ”
come pouring into the platter of “Abraca-
dabra” that, seeing that fifty pounds divided
among 1000 successful literary persons would only
run to a bob each, he wisely concludes that the
£5O and the sixpences will receive more intelligent
handling while in his possession than in that of
any of his numerous correspondents who vainly
await “your cheque by return of post.” Then
“Abracadabra” changes his name to “ Philan-
thropist,” seeks a new address and’calls for solu-
tions of the letters “ Chutton-mops ” next week.

* * *
A certain Australian judge went to Eng-
land some time ago suffering from severe illness
the result of overwork. When lie arrived in BuH-
land his state of health became worse than ever
and, having been assured on most eminent medical
authority that it was quite unlikely he would ever
recover, he ante himself down and indited a long
and dismal letter of final farewell to his brother-
judges, in which he said, among other rather
sultry things, that though he could not hope to be
remembered as a great lawyer or as a great judge,
ho hoped to be thought of as one who had always
conscientiously striven to do his duty and had ever
tried to act as a courteous and kindly gentleman
(the fact being that His Honor had always tried
to do the square thing, but had been celebrated for
a darned snappy temper—mainly caused by ill-
health). The Chief Justice got the letter in course
of post, and then handed it to Mr. Justice Vento-
sior, who snorted characteristically and passed it
on to his junior. Byand-bye the body of the
judge came along to Australia, not in a box, but
in the saloon of the mail-steamer. It was in first-
class working-order, and accompanied by its soul.
It now takes its little three meals a day, and that
it may long continue to do so is the sincere wish of
yours truly, for the judge is really not half a bad
sort. However, it will be long ere his 0. J. and
MV. Justice Ventosior cease to chaff their learned
colleague about his last dying speech and confes-
sion. Moral: Never write your own obituary.

* * «
A good deal depends on the way you put a
a thing. How is this, for example, from the
Church Times
On 17th instant, promoted from the Kindergarten of
earth to the High School of Paradise, the sweet soul
of Mary Sophia Horsley, wife of the Rev. J. VV.
Horsley, Holy Trinity, Woolwich.
There is genuine poetry in that little “ ad.” .And
yet the world is full of grovelling wretches who,-
with equally good material to work upon, will go
out and advertise that Ann Smith died owing to
something going wrong with her stomach. Why
is there not more soul in this world?
* * *
As the result of excluding the Bible from
State schools, says a reverend person named Eer-
guson, “Government railways are defrauded,
banks are robbed by absconding clerks, and forged
cheques are issued by educated thieves and
systematic swindlers.” Just so. And yet the
gigantic City of Glasgow Bank swindle was
brought about by a directorate of pious elders,
and the last broken bank in Australia went to
eternal smash through too much Y.M.C.A. in the
management, and two or three clergymen were
bailed up for travelling without tickets the other
day in Melbourne, and a good many people well
up in Scripture seem to have made an unlawful
rise of late. Is there anything in the mere fact
of a man being named Ferguson that is destructive
to his reasoning powers, we wonder?
* * #
One of the burning questions in Northern
ecclesiastical circles again is : May a visiting
clergyman, under any circumstance, decorously
place his hand beneath the arm of a female hos-
pital patient ? One reverend gentleman stoutly
declares that such is possible. The general public,
however, maintain that the doctors don’t require
any professional assistance from the clergy.

We read that Mr. A. W. Robertson, of
Mount Morgan fame, who has leased Lord Aber-
deen’s house, 27, Grosvenor-square (alongside the
London mansions of the Duke of Westminster and
Sir Salmon Wilson), “gave a brilliant reception and
concert in celebration of the coming out of his
daughters, the Misses Robertson, at which the
Duke of Edinburgh and a large proportion of the
British peerage were present, while Australians
naturally mustered in great force. The whole
affair was by far the most magnificent and
luxurious that London has seen for several seasons.
Even Colonel North, with all his extravagant dis-
play, has never excelled it. Madame Melba, who
is now in London, and is constituting a great
attraction at the opera, was present, and, we are
told, received the fee of 200 guineas for her trifling
though melodious vocal exertions. Indeed, the
musical part of the entertainment alone absorbed
very nearly £l5OO, while the flowers represented
quite a modest fortune.” The old, old story :
Champagne in a golden bucket in Hingland—
flour in a battered pannikin (and sometimes not
that) in Australia.

Judge Denniston, of N.Z., lately refused
his discharge to a workman, who had gone broke
in a humble way for £5O, on the ground that the
bankrupt was still in possession of “ the only asset
lie ever had-his labour.” Yet the insolvent
mining boon: man, whose only asset was the loan
of somebody else’s name on the, back of a bill and
a boundless poufer of tying, is allowed to retain
these properties and start afresh ; and the busted
doctor, who started business with a great stock of
ignorance and a bottle of ointment with’a Hy in
it, seems to get through all right; and the-de-
famtmg lawyer gets his discharge, and retains the
on y assets he ever had-a capacity for cursing a
witness all the way to the bottomless pit and back
again, and for making off with the trust-money in
between times. There seem« tu be a discrepancy
somewhere.
* * *
At a recent swell marriage in Paris the
Pope’s blessing for the bridal brace was put in the
form of a telegram which a priest brought to the
officiating Nuncio upon a silver salver. Where-
upon the Nuncio read the sacred “wire,” just as
though he were surprised to see it, and the bride’s
mamma went into the vestry and anted up ten
thousand francs for the glad tidings. Now The
Bulletin will telegraph its blessing to any
newly-married pair for a year’s subscription, paid
in advance,

Cairns editors are now threatened with
thrashings if they publish the names of drunks.
Cairns must be getting civilised. When we were
in Cairns people were proud of being drunk.

* * #
An experienced hand was sent as a reporter
to the Melbourne Medical Association, there
being some culinary subject on. He saw a quan-
tity of preserved meat, on willow-pattern plates
upon a sideboard, and the experienced rubbed his
hands—supper coming off probably. Presently a
doctor pointed to the meat, saying that it was"an
interesting collection of abnormal livers, taken
from human subjects.
* * *
The Victorian Volunteer force are prac-
tising at a “disappearing target.” Perhaps after
a few years of this they may grow proficient
enough to bring down the disappearing bank-
manager, who is so common in these parts. We
don’t know any more fleeting and volatile target
than he is.

An Auckland (N.Z.) telegraph-operator
has filed his schedule, the reason of the disaster
being that he was “induced to endorse bills for
his stepfather. Any person who is on the verge
of going security for his mother-in-law, or who
thinks of lending a large sum to his deceased
wife s sister, may take warning by this unhappy
incident. J
* * *
Mary Ann King, a Melbourne lady, who
holds a half-share in a gentleman now under ar-
rest for bigamy, stated in her evidence that she
advertised for a kindred soul, and received 32
applications for the situation. She selected the
defendant as the most promising of the lot, from
which we would assume that the other 31 must
have been a gallows-looking crowd, with homicide
and incendiarism and abandonment of helpless
infants written large on their beetle brows. The
police-force had a long and exciting chase after
Mary s husband, and he was trapped at last
through making an appointment with that injured
lady. Even then it seems he had time to bang
Mary Ann about very considerably before the
detective jumped on him and wrenched him off
and if that official had been a moment later there
would probably have been an utterly ruined Ann
lying on the ground, and the case would have
assumed a new aspect. The great law that man
must either bust or advertise evidently fails to
apply to a lone woman in search of a husband
She advertises first and busts afterwards.

A dogs’ -tea-party is the last bloated in-
sanity from the United States. It was first
introduced by the American Pet Dog Club, or the
Overfed Pigs’ Association, or some similar body,
and is fast becoming popular-. Cards of invitation
are sent round to the dogs, care of their owners,
and some fashionable idiot disguised as a female
comes to chapel-one each canine. Turkey-bones,
coffee, and ice-cream are served on hassocks, and a
Japanese napkin is attached to every beast’s
oollai’. After the meal is over rose-coloured
fin°-er-bowls, filled with scented water, are handed
round, and the dogs are expected to dabble their
paws in them, with their mistresses’ assistance.
The wealthy American lady of to-day is generally
either dry-nurse and foot-washer to an expensive
mongrel, or else she is a female-doctor, and
refreshes herself by dissecting legs and arms and
disembowelling corpses; or, if she is neither of
these, she is a professor, and goes about lecturing
olx colic and entrails. Is our civilisation a failure ?
We rather think it is.

amateur.poet the other day—suburban-paper poet,
you know—and he asked me, says lie, "What do
you think of this little effort of mine?” I read it.
“ The metre is jerky,” says I; “and, by the way,
your s always is.” “ Well, yoxi see, I can’t help it,
under the circumstances.” “How's that?”
“ Well, you know, we can’t afford fires, and, as
night is my time of inspiration, during this beastly
cold weather I’m obliged to compose all my poetxy
in bed.” “ What’s that got to do with the jerki-
ness of the metre?” “Do? All to do! The
wife’s d foghorn erratic snoring governs the
rhythm /’’—Yours, The Dipsomaniac.
The new Lady Weymouth, lately presented
at Court by her aunt, the Countess of Dudley, the
loveliest member of the lovely Moncreiff family,
is the daughter of Viciet Mordaunt, and the cir-
cumstances of her birth in 1870 made it impossible
that Sir Charles Mordaunt could be her father,
while the confession of her mother distributed the
paternal responsibility among three rakish persons
Sir Frederick Johnstone, Viscount Cole, and
the present Prince of Wales. “ Tummy ” after-
wards denied in court having had any finger in the
pie by simply saying “ No ” in reply to the briefly-
worded question of a barrister. Any other man
would have been subjected to cross-examination—
how delicious it would have been to see Tummy
put through his facings by a Purves! —and
there is little doubt that everyone in court
stood up, or that the judge removed his wig while
His Mightiness briefly occupied the witness-box.
Sir Charles obtained his divorce; Cole and John-
stone being pronounced particeps crirninis, and he
afterwards refused to see the little girl who
legally bore his name, and she was adopted by her
mother’s sister, the Countess of Dudley. Though
almost blind at her birth (her infirmity was
brought as evidence against the debauchees who
had taken advantage of her mother’s weakness
and Mox-daunt’s absence in Norway), she ulti-
mately gained perfect vision, and became such a
peerless beauty that she was, although dowexless,
the rage of last season, and speedily captivated
Weymouth, the heir of the wealthy Earl
of Bath, who refused a short lime ago
the hand of Princess Mary—now bride-elect
of Prince Edward of Wales —for his son, on the
ground that the Marquis of Bath could never con-
sent to appear in a social rank subordinate to that
of Ins wife, as in, for instance, the cases of the
the Duke of Fife and the Marquis of Lome. Hex-
mother, Violet Moncreiff, lives almost entirely in
Paris, and is now known to be a confirmed morpho-
iiianiao.

# * *
The United States census conies off this
year, and among the other questions in the census
paper the citizen is requested to state frankly
whether he is blind, maimed, crippled, paralysed,
or short of any of his senses, whether he is insane
or diseased, and whether he is a convict, a home-
less child, or a pauper. Also he is expected to
mention if his property is mortgaged and several
other things. And further, it is provided that if
he declines to supply information on any of these
subjects the census-taker may fill up the deficiency
by facta gathered in the neighbourhood —in other
words, he is authorised- to ask the woman next
door. Anybody who.fully understands the in-
grained malignity—-the base vulgarity—the whole-
souled meanness of the female in the next house
may form the first beginnings of a rudimentary
idea of the horrors concealed, in that last proviso.
After an experience’extending over 30 years and
covering the four quarters of the globe we have
come to the conclusion that there are few low
and degraded things that the woman next door is
not capable of. She is a person of grovelling in-
stincts and bad manners, and she throws her rub-
bish oyer the fence. She has eleven children, and
she spanks them all with a board in the back
yard. She is ignorant and soulless, and has no
poetry in her composition, and she spends most of
her existence in banging doors and slamming
dishes about. Nothing that we know of has done
more to demoralise society and to bring humanity
down to the level of the lower animals than the
baleful influence of the person next door, and the
further continuance of the female on the adjacent
premises is a menace to human progress which
ought to be abolished by law. Whether Hie is
naturally objectionable, or whether she begins to
degenerate, through the operation of some obscure
law of nature, on the day when she moves into
the adjoining house, are points that have never-
been determined, but experience has proved that
the individual in the contiguous residence is one
of the most conspicuous.blights on our civilisation,
and if she is not suppressed by prompt legislation
the time must come when the masses will rise
unanimously and demand her abolition. Man is
only a temporary institution at the best. He is
composed chiefly of hair, drink, bad grammar,
tobacco-smoke, indignation and swear-words, and,
being built of these fleeting and transient materials,
he has not time to wait till the woman next door
dies out in the ordinary process of nature. There-
fore, he will certainly erect himself on his own legs
sooner or later, to promote her downfall; and now
that the law allows her to fill in his census-paper
for him, and describe him as a person of defective
intellect, and put him down as ■ a pauper and a
Chinaman, a howl will go up to the listen'ng
heavens and a voice will be heard crying for the
abolition of the feminine obstruction in the adjoin-
ing premises, and the woman next door will be
wiped out by the burning wrath of an outraged
nation.

Manvoocl, the deceased hangman, who now
dwells serenely on the Evei’green Shore, died
insolvent. His professional instincts were too
much for him, and he went broke in his declining
years over a speculation in hemp. When a man
is so much eaten up. by his trade as that he is
always liable to come to a bad end. A respectable
and honourable hangman, as a rule, keeps clear of
the “shop” except when-he is actually hanging,
and seeks rather to cultivate the harmless dicky-
bird or to grow the refreshing violet, and to find
his dolighf.hr the simple Works Of nature. If any
one happens by chance to come across a stranger
lost in profound admiration of an unpretentious
herb he is three times more likely to be a hang-
man than a poet, and it is a dead certainty that
he isn’t a printer; and on the other hand if you
chance to observe a person in a plain black suit
who floors eight coal-heavers in succession the
probabilities are that he is either a curate or an
undertaker far gone in consumption. The hang-
man is generally Understood to be a good husband
and father, though in his affection for children he
is probably inferior to the old stoker of the In-
quisitiou, and regarded as a pleasant, gentlemanly
companion in private life he falls behind the
person who used to earn an honest livelihood by
pouring the boiling lead over the heretic. In pro-
found knowledge, too, he cannot compare with
the Chinese philosopher who bamboos the witness
and hangs him up by the hair in order to make
hjm speak the truth, but then this individual is
generally a university graduate and has devoted
all his early years to literature ; but, on the whole,,
the British hangman is a person of a peaceful turn
of mind, and when he sits in his cottage in'the
evening and sings a pleasant little hymn to hiin-
seif the local clergyman appears a low, ordinary,,
worldly individual by comparison. Only the
hangman, as a rule, is not a successful business;
man, and when he speculates in hemp in his old
age the results are apt to be bad for his creditors,,
and he has to be buried by the parish. Marwoods;
only asset when he died was a bad debt for hang-
ffig two assassins in Dublin, a circumstance which
shows clearly that all executions should take place
ou a cash basis with a discount of 2i ner cent, and
that a bill at three months should! be promptly
rejected unless there are two good names on tin;'
back.

Mr. “Tommy” Dangar, M.P. (N.S.W.),
who died the other day, was very fond of a prac-
tical joke. lie was. once out with a mustering
party which included a new-ehnm jaekeroo, whir
fancied that he couldn’t possibly be “ had.” They
were camped alongside a creek running about
breast-high, and the Britisher was taking a nap
‘ inside the tent when he heard a big hullabaloo,
and, rushing out, saw on the other side of the
creek Mr. Dangar, with upraised axe, standing
over a man whose head and body were bathed in.
gore. Johnny from England, ignoring the fallen
tree a bit further down the creek, dashed through
the water, in horrified tones begging Dangar to
desist, and it was not till A loud guffaw from the
people in hiding was heard that the poor jaekeroo
knew that he had soaked his only decent togs
for nought. The man on the ground had been
most artistically painted with red gum.

The Land, Trade,
• —AND—
Labour Union of Australia*
Has been established. Its objects are :
L To unite all the above classes in one body, but
with respect to shearers it is intended to apply
to them as station hands, which very many of
them often arc.
2. To raise wages, to obtain better tucker, and
men’s huts being supplied.
3. Generally to advocate the interests of its mem-
bers. The strength of the Union will during
next general shearing be put forth in support
of the above objects. That the Union is a
fighting one and bona fide is proved in the
strike against Messrs. Chaffey Bros., Limited.
Men, come and join us ! Though only lately
started we have proved the metal we are made
of, as the public are aware from the late
reports in the Press.

■ ,-jps are always unfavourably handicapped for Sydney
t he New South Waters get in lightly at Flemington
and f'aulfleld Whatever reason there may be for this
jaiht it is obviously good business policy on the
of the Victorian managements to give “foreign
PjLg a fair inducement to come over, just as any
systematic overweighting of visitors must prejudice sport
on the Sydney side.

# * *
Steeplechase Pay at Elemington was a rousing
success in point of attendance, Stand, Hill, and Flat—
osnpciallv the latter-showing respectable imitations of
dm Melbourne Cup crowds. Probably there were 20,000
upoblb to curse the fate that brought them to.the meet-
in<r With the exception of Bendigo, whose biggest
hackers came from N.S.W., not one “good thing” came
off durin" the afternoon, and the books, who lost heavily
on minor’events the preceding Saturday, wore au almost
unbroken smile. The local sporting writers could sec no
virtue in Fearless 11., although the horse had won in bnl-
lian style the first day and was bound to have, as The
Bulletin sii° r ii*GstGclj cigpood show. In the t&cg hG mnet g his
own running and won all the way, only the old, often-
tried performers managing to get round after him.
Of the “ private reputation ” animals Busaco was the most
utter failure. ‘Credited with being the best jumper in the
universe he fell three times. Lawler, who looked in fine
form probably came to grief through being too light-
hearted while Titokawaru, who certainly finished second,
iumped’sluggishly throughout. The stewards called upon
the owner of Bendigo to explain the strange improvement
in his horse’s form since the previous Saturday, but a
very remarkable piece of riding in the Open Steeplechase
escaped their notice. Only the public noticed. Mel-
bourne has now reverted to its normal condition, and
horse-talk is laid aside until October, when the preliminary
Caulfield Cup fever, deepening into the “Carnival”
frenzy, will break out once more.
* * *
Melbourne Sportsman’s leading tipster selected
(only three days beforehand) three horses for the Grand
National Hurdle Race. Not one of them was a starter.
# * *
Surefoot has shown that his Derby running was
all wrong, by subsequently beating the Oaks winner,
Memoir.
# * *
Fuller Pilch, the old-time cricketer, said that
he liked always to have at least one wild spirit in his
eleven who would go in and hit ’em all over the shop.
Poor Bates filled this billet for several of our English
visiting teams, and was a valuable gate-money player in
consequence.
* * *
The foreigners take the cake for jumping prizes.
The Auteuil (France) Grand Steeplechase is worth £SOOO,
and die Hurdle Race has £2OOO added, being £SOO more
than was given to the recent Grand Nationals at Fleming-
ten But the V.R.C. will be standing £SOOO for their
Winter Steeplechase in about another three years, judging
from appearances.
# * *
Rev. Dr. Bevan supports the totallsator. That’s
to say, he prefers it to betting. The Doctor gradually
brings out his Socialism, like the Scotch piper, called upon
by the Hibernian to “ Jist play the ‘ Byne Wather’ jintly,
and see how I can shtand it.”
* * *
In the football field on Saturday, N.S.W. v.
Queensland, mud was the order of the day. Sydney
dressed down the visitors, the most prominent in the field
again being Lusk and Frazer, the latter’s performances
being specially remarkable on account of the heavy
ground and his immense bulk. Queensland had all the
best of the “ scrums,” but it must have been owing to
condition, as they did not appear to have weight on their
side. The wet ground was against anything like scientific
football, hut with a fine week better things may be looked
for next Saturday, when the teams renew the battle.
* * *
Delayed cable-news coming in the lump inform
us that the cricket-match against Gloucestershire was
drawn greatly in favour of the Australians; that the
Players defeated “Our Boys” at Sheffield, in a return
match, by five wickets ; and that the match with Surrey
would have resulted, if not drawn, in a sound trouncing
for Australia. When going to press the match with All
England, at Lord’s, was being closely contested, but the
scores were small. The cricketers have decided to abandon
the proposed Indian and African tours.
* * *
The writer received a letter from one of the Aus-
tralian Eleven recently which said that Sam Jones was out
of form, but was “coming on,” which prophecy has since
been verified by Jones’ cabled scores. Murdoch was
batting splendidly, also Ferris. The bowlers were in great
form, but the writer complained bitterly that—British
fair play again—“in every match they had to play the
umpire.”
# * *
To an outsider it would seem strange how lightly
the English press has let down the present Australian
Eleven. Previous and successful teams were money-
grabbers, professionals, and not even fit for the Gentlemen
of England to lunch with. This team are fair cherubim
with the English press. J. Bull always did like anybody
he could lick.
* * *
Rev. Editor Pitchett, of Melbourne Daily
Telegraph, meets the Devil half-way—by giving full
reports of horse and other races without any mention, of
the odds. “Is gambling forbidden in the BibleV” is a
modern debating society question. Well, is it? Perhaps
in the spirit, but not in the letter. So you can safely
write to the Behemoth sweep, with a present of £l, on
All Fools’ Day.
# * *
The late Lord Derby never gave his winning
jockeys anything more than the regular fee. Butler won
a big race for him, and was overjoyed when the Earl gave
him a clutch of game-fowls’ eggs, but his mates sub-
stituted duck eggs for them, and Butler was aghast, at
the result with his game ducks.
* * *
by the time this appears in print “Billy’-
Murphy, the phenomenal feather-weight boxer, will have
again put foot on Australian soil. In America Murphy
beat the best men at his weight that England and America
could produce, and he returns laden with scalps and
dollars. He is one of the few sensible athletes who look
forward to the day when their strength and cunning shall
have deserted them, which seems much to have annoyed
the Yankees. “Griffo” intends, to have a shy at the
champion, but, clever as the lad undoubtedly is, the
writer does not think he has a big show with the slaugh-
tering Murphy,
* # #
The horse export, Melbourne to India, this
year, is particularly lively, and 2000 is said to be the
total. One ship took 390 last week. The very cream of
our horses, the most useful sort, go to mount the
Bengal cavalry and other equestrian Sepoy sabreurs and
lancers.
* # #
Melbourne Steeplechase meeting brings again
jockey slaughter on the tapis, though, as it happens, they
didn’t exactly kill any this time. There is great reckless-
ness in putting jocks on old crocks with three legs and a
swinger, for steeples. How seldom do you hear of any
serious accident in a race like the Liverpool Grand
National! The slain jockey record of Australia is
ghastly and devilish, this “Butchered to make a
Roman holiday ” business. Why have jockeys no Trade-
Union ?
■* * #
John Eden Savill has leased the racehorse
Chicago for two years in England. Ringmaster and
Lady Betty have not added to their successes on the
English turf, but Mr. Dan. Cooper’s two-year-old Ally
Dorcas has won the Princess of Wales’ Cup at the New-
market July meeting.
* # #
Charley Samuels, the famous aboriginal pedes-
trian, has" fallen low and quitted the running-track.
Starvation in Sydney has driven him to the aboriginal
camp at Botany. This prompts us to inquire about the
men who are alleged to have won fabulous sums over the
darkey as well as those who were so enthusiastic in
cheering his victories.
# # *
William O’Connor, having got over the gloom
consequent on his defeat by Stanbury, which caused him
to decide that he’d never row again, challenges M‘Lean
to row him over the Nepean course for £2OO. O’Connor,
like Hanlan, doesn’t fancy the currents and tides 1 of the
Parramatta.
The Literary World, London, by last
mail, reviews four Australian books of poetry by
four different poets, in one batch.
Temple Bar has a yarn of man who fell
down a New Zealand geyser and was shot up
again —of course—or where would the yarn be ?
In associating the N.S.-W. Postmaster-
General with barmaids, the Protestant Standard
almost “ travelled from Dan to Beershebeens.”

Extract from a gaol billet-doux, read in the
police-court last week: —“I went over toe your
old woman and giv hir the muney for your cloze.
She told us the interest would come to 5 shillings,
so I had to pome my kote to giv hir the muney.”
We hope the old woman lent him the kote to re
place the one he porned, because the real owner oi
the cloze was still in “ jug ” at the time and
couldn’t possibly be wanting to do a mash. Alto-
gether it was a mixed transaction, from whicl
“uncle ” came out top, as usual.
A London newspaper devotes a column to
the new papyrus of the Egyptian “ Book of the
Dead,” found at Thebes, and dating from about
Moses’s time. It appears to show that Christianity
existed even then, for the parallelism with our own
beliefs and worship is simply amazing, as Pro-
fessor Denton showed with his lectures on Egypt
in Australia. We remember Bishop Moorhouse
attending one and coming out much impressed.
Poor old Denton, it will be remembered, lies in a
New Guinea grave. Once he held a public dis-
cussion with Garfield, afterwards U.S. President*

critic and composer, skilled book-keeper. Address,
H., 116 Bathurst-street, Sydney,
A Barcoo squatters’ organ, formulating a
new code of rules to be adopted by the Shearers’
Union, jokingly suggests this: —“No shearer is
expected to have less than five or more than ten
horses with him, and as many goats as he likes.”

Allcock’s Porous Plasters.
—IN—
Chest, Liver, and Kidney Affections.
St. Veryan Vicarage, Gram pound-road,
Cornwall, January, 1890.
Dear Sir,—I write to inform you that your
“ Allcock’s Porous Plasters ” are excellent—a real
blessing. Whatever I may say, I feel I cannot
praise them sufficiently. I have given away
hundreds amongst the poor and suffering, and 1
cannot recall a single instance where they failed
to give relief. They have been used by me for
| complaints in the chest, liver, kidneys, and spine,
I with marked results, in fact, I know of three
special cases,, out of many (one of chest, one of
liver, one of kidneys) where the sufferers had been
to the infirmary, tried many doctors, without any
benefit, and were told that nothing more could be
done. I gave them your plasters, and after a fair
trial of them the result was really wonderful.
The sufferer in the chest no longer complains of
i weakness, has lost all distressing cough, and is
I quite strong. The sufferer from liver complaint
! no longer complains of indigestion and pains in
the side and between the shoulders, but is quite a
different person. The sufferer from kidney com-
plaint no longer lies in bed nine months in the
year in agonies, but is able to get out of doors
every day, and is free from pain. I shall recom-
mend these plasters wherever I go.—With all
good wishes for the New Year, yours very grate-
fully,
JAMES ARTHUR KEMP, Minister.

NEW REMEDY FOR IRISH
DIFFICULTIES.
A leading Dublin paper, having heard so much
said in both the Irish and English press in favour
of a wonderful oil, determined to investigate the
matter by personal interviews with the people
who had used it, in order to learn whether or not
one half that was said of its magical power wa s
true, or whether it was one of those gigantic
advertising schemes, the magnitude of which in
these modern times fairly takes one’s breath
away. Its representative interviewed a number
of people in various parts of Ireland who had
been cured by the application of the oil, and in
every instance he found that the published state-
ments were true and correct in every particular.
Everywhere people were enthusiastic in its praise.
A captain of the Royal Irish Fusiliers, who had
suffered long from rheumatism and sprained
ankle, said: “The effect after three applications
of St. Jacobs oil was simply marvellous ; the pain
at once departed, and has never returned.”
A prominent hotel proprietor, after suffering
years from rheumatic gout, said: “It would be
hard to conceive of anything working a cure so
rapidly as St. Jacobs oil did in my ease.”
A sergeant of the Royal Irish Constabulary
told of a friend who had been a great sufferer for
years with rheumatism in the back, suffering great
pain ; using two bottles of St. Jacobs oil the pain
entirely disappeared, and never returned.
A young woman in Castlebar told the reporter
of the almost miraculous cure which her mother,
aged 70 years, had experienced from using the
invaluable St. Jacobs oil. She had been almost
helpless for two years from rheumatism, and was
completely cured by using less than one bottle of
the oil.
A prominent surgeon in Tipperary related the
case of a parish priest, who had for a long time
suffered the most intense agony from sciatica
completely cured in three days by rubbing him’
self well with St. Jacobs oil.
A very enthusiastic Irishman in Gort was met
with, shouting “Long five St. Jacobs oil.” He
had suffered the most intense rheumatic pains for
years, and was cured by using the contents of one
bottle of the oil.
A cooper in Beale, County Kerry, Ireland,
related how his knee was twice its natural size,
stiff, and painful, and he was unable to stand,
swelling and pain entirely disappeared after using
St. Jacobs oil one week.
An old gentleman in Mallow was cured in one
week after having commenced to use St. Jacobs
oil, of severe rheumatism of six years’ standing.
On returning to Dublin, the reporter called on
some of the leading chemists, for the purpose o
learning their views. Messrs. R. Simpson an .
Co., of 16, Henry-street, the well-known chemists®
stated that St. Jacobs oil was the most popular>
remedy now being sold ; that it is no uncommon
thing for them to sell 1200 bottles of the oil in a
single week. That their last order was for 600
dozen, or 7200 bottles, and you can rest assured,
said Mr. Simpson, the senior partner, that no
medicine would have this enormous sale unless .it
possessed actual merit. -
PROGRESS IN CIVILISATION.
Nature never makes a mistake. Whatever
material is at hand is turned to the best use.
Man, the creation of Nature, follows in her foot-
steps, and steadily seeks a higher plane by reject-
ing the less worthy, and selecting that at his com-
mand which is the most practical. This progress
in civilisation is marked in all details of life, and
particularly in the different apparatus or mechani-
cal contrivances for every day contingencies. One
by one the less fitting drop* out, or are literally
crowded aside by those offering superior advan-
tages. Steam has superseded sail. The old time
horological contrivances are pushed to the rear by
the newer, less costly, and more reliable appliances
of to-day. To-day "the public demand economys
combined with quality, honesty, and Accuracy, and,
in all these particulars the Waterbury watches
“fill the bill,” and thereby have gained respect
and admiration in every quarter of the earth as
honest, reliable, and faithful timekeepers, pro-
duced at as low a cost as is compatible with'good
material, skilful workmanship, and ■ their un-
doubtedly excellent qualifications'.
CAUTION.— AII Watches Manufactured
by the Waterbury Watch Co. have the word
“WATERBURY” printed in fine type upon
THEIR DIAL FACES, AND ALSO THE INTERWOVEN'
LETTERS W.W.CO., WITHOUT WHICH NO WATCH
sold as a WATERBURY is genuine. If you
want correct time and a: watch which in event of
accident can be efficiently repaired at a reasonable
cost, DEMAND THE WATERBURY.

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest

cated, with these widely recognised qualification
combined with unswerving faithfulness to hij
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. llis practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes. Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back, Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a

blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking

BRUCE SMITH.
Answers to Correspondents.
“An Australian” (Riley-street): You are evi-
dently an Englishman, like the critic of whom you com-
plain. It is because he is a Londoner and knows his
subject that he slates your idols, with confidence, when
they require it. Now, you do not know your subject.
You ask why shows that are coldly received in Melbourne
should in Sydney prove highly successful ? There are
about sixteen reasons. Melbourne, it has to be admitted,
is the theatrical metropolis of Australia, with five theatres
furnishing strong entertainments at one time. Theatrical
managers say that Sydney seldom has more than one
theatre doing big business, the favoured one being rushed.
Sydney gets one at a time the “ fashionable ” shows which
Melbourne has in bunches “Yussif”: It is true that
emigrants to Canada and young natives of a certain age
are entitled to take up 160 acres of land free of cost, al-
most unhampered by conditions; but we read that a
lately-made provision hands over another hundred acres
to the man who has a family of eleven children.... “ J.O.’’
(Harden): Not vitriolic enough....“ Niels ” (Gvmpie) ;
Thanks.. “M.W.S.” : “ Innocents at Home ” rather long..
“Box 291”: We are only bound by our published con-
ditions. We can’t tell until we see your MS “P. L.”:
Thanks.E.L.A.”; There’s little ground for hysterics.
A thorough inquiry into the Vernon management may
reassure the community ; in any case no harm can be
done by investigation “E.M.0.”: The Bulletin does
not respect age to the extent which you so dogmati*
cally prescribe. The high official named sets himself up
as a fair target for political criticism by continuing to
exercise on occasions the power of life or death. Why
should a man who is so powerful be exempt from criti-
cism ? Were he altogether retired the case would,
perhaps, be somewhat different “ W.G. ” (Bomba): Ah
old yarn already printed in Bulletin “ Nhagilliac ” :
Under consideration.... “ P.R.D.” (Adelaide) : Yes, under
(he conditions stated in every issue “R.P.W.”: Try
your hand again. What you send is altogether too flimsy,
but it tends to show that you could, with care, write ex-
cellent verse...,“P. Hobson”: “Prodigal Son” really
humorous, but too wordy, and disfigured by repetitions. ..
“Constant Reader” (Townsville): Duval and Fenton’s
“Artistic Anatomy ”is the best. We dare say Turner and
Henderson, Sydney, would send it you for about 65..,.
“ Martin Robertson ”: You have totally overlooked one
weighty factor. If rents be controlled by prices, it by no
means follows that a fall in prices, which induces a fall in
rent, will revolutionise the position of the landlord towards
the community. His rent-returns are lowered, you say.
\ es, but the purchasing power of money is also increased
by that fall in prices which lowered those rents. Thus
the smaller sum represents to him as much comfort as
before, and, wages being also lowered, his tenants will
find it Just as hard as ever to meet his demands

The Escaped Convict.
“ The Frenchman’s gone!” so ran the tale
One week ago through Pentridge gaol,
Told by a warder deathly pale.
Pierre Douar had gone once more—
Not by the route he chose before,
When in the gloom of stormy night
The dauntless Frenchman first took flight,
From cage so strong, o’er wall so steep,
With fearless heart and fearless leap,
Away to the city enwrapped in sleep,
There were locks to pick and walls to scale
When Pierre Douar first broke from gaol.
They brought him back —the story old
Of a comrade false and a comrade sold ;
They bore him off to a dreary cell,
To the black despair of a noiseless hell,
To think of “ the God who freely gave
His own dear Son lost man to save; ”
To think and to bless with uplifted hand
The Christlike law of a Christian land ;
Seeing the light for an hour each day,
Silently watching the sunbeams play
For sixty minutes, then back to—pray ;
Bearing the strain till his brain gave way.
What did he mean by that surly moan—
“My body is their’s, but my soul’s my own?” .
Why did he fall on his trembling knees
And pray the Inspector prayers like these,
He, Frenchman Douar, who knew no fear—
“ Take me away, O away from here ! ”
“ Take me away, for the love of God ! ”
Coming from him it was strangely odd.
The ‘‘System’s ” good for a convict bad—■
Who is to know when it drives him mad ?
“ The Frenchman’s gone !” so ran the tale
Told by a warder deathly pale.
His body lay still on the cell’s cold stone,
But gone was the spirit he called his own.
(For never a system yet could check
The flight of a man with a broken neck)
The Devil, perchance, had heard his prayer
And taught him the way to escape from there,
To rescue himself with unaided hand
From the damning law of a Christian laud.
Bury it down in an ill-dug hole,
The Frenchman’s body—we’ve lost his soul.
The law will curse, on reflecting how
He is probably chatting with Satan now,
Enjoying the fire, and feeling well
As he never felt in the noiseless Hell.
And he laughs at the notion of walls to scale —
There’s a shorter way out of Pentridge gaol.
The editor of a Philadelphia newspaper
has contributed £20 towards a fund for purchasing
and preserving Wordsworth’s cottage at Grass-
mere. One of these days the Yanks will buy
Shakespeare’s house for a million sterling and
ship it over to Boston.
It is now an acknowledged fact in London
that it is difficult, if not impossible, to tell the
difference between a prime New Zealand leg of
mutton and that cut from an ordinary English
sheep, and of this the butchers take advantage,
for one seldom costs more than 5d. per lb. whole-
sale, while for the other they would have to pay
8d. per lb.
The Solway Patent Tumhle-over Box
Wool press.
xhis Woolpress is made by T. Robinson and Co.,
Ltd., of Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, and is a
simple and light press of new design, but at the
same time well-tried, and its advantages make it
the coming press for small woolgrowers. As the
accompanying illustration will show, the pressure
is obtained by two short iron levers, each having
two pauls, one retaining, the other releasing, and
these working (one on each side) in a wrought iron
ratchet bar with a loop at the top; this loop is
placed over a strong tree iron plate bolted to the
monkey, or press head, the raising of the levers
lift the releasing or upper paul, and the lowering
engages the paul mentioned, till the operator again
lifts the lever, when the retaining or bottom paul
instantly engages with the ratchet bar and holds
the pressure, and so on, till the bale is sufficiently
pressed. One great advantage attached to this
system is that when the pressure is first placed
upon the wool the resistance is slight and the
operator or operators on the levers may lift them
high and press low at eacli reach so that several
notches may be
put down at each
reach, and as the
resistance in-
creases and to the
finish, a short
stroke is given
and levers made
to maintain about
the position
shown, so that
the pressers may
be able comfortably to put their whole weight
and strength upon them. Avery short stroke
of the levers will enable one notch at
least to be taken. Both the levers project
in the same direction, so that when necessary
one man can operate both at once (one in each
hand) and alone perfectly press a bale. With the '
bale still in, the top portion of press can be turned
down ii)ion its hinges for refilling, while the front-
door and a half door at the back are made to open
so that the bale cap may bo quickly and handily
sown. The advantages claimed are that it
requires in setting; it will stand on the ground
or on any flat spot; for inside working it takes
very little room ; both top and bottom boxes are
filled from the floor ; no staging is required; its
leverage is so great that boys can work it and also
understand it, or when required a man can (single-
Handed) take it asunder and load it in a dray.
This is an important thing amongst small growers,
as one press may be made to do several sheds, and
its most important advantage is that it will press
quicker than any other yet made, and is therefore
alike good for the largest wool-growers. It weighs
in all about Bewt, and price £22 10s.
An attempt to cross the cat with the fox
is reported to have resulted in the production of
an animal with a fox’s head and a cat’s tail, which
combined the bad qualities of both animals. This
specimen of zoology flourished for some time near
Jerilderie (N.S.W.), but was recently executed on
a charge of becoming too expensive. It is a pity
experiment can’t be carried a little further. A
hybrid between a solicitor and an octopus, for in-
stance, would be interesting and instructive; and
a cross between a preacher who is interested in
land syndicates and a bill of sale at 120 per shent
might throw quite anew light on some obscure
moral problems. Also a considerable amount of
interest would attach to the experiment, if it were
possible, to mate a Polynesian missionary with an
’error out of the Evening Noose, and then sit
down and study results.
The difficulties of Thomas Owen, of Mel-
bourne, don’t appear to be so serious as was at first
supposed. He is only being harassed a little about
two chai’ges of shoplifting, nine of housebreaking,
three of assault and robbery, three of stealing
from the person, four of stealing from dwellings,
two of burglary, seven of ordinary theft, one little
case of sacrilege, and two small matters about
bolting out of shops with goods which he hadn’t
paid for. Also one man is raising a considerable
dust about 185 books which were found in Mr.
Owen’s shop, and another is rioting around more
or less on the subject of 31 overcoats. Also some
o'her people seem to have given way to fleshly
passions on matters relating to several hundred
watches, about 40 rings, one gun, seven chisels,
two pairs of spectacles, two saws, and about six
tons of other articles, and away back at the rear
of the procession a person has departed from his
normal state of holy calm in relation to enough
opera glasses and telescopes to start an optician
in a large way of business. Otherwise Mr. Owen
is all right.
“ When you I am Yar away from me,
Donald, drink Innes and Grieve’s fine, old-
matured, and mellow whisky every evening at

your lips touch the quaigh you will know that I,
