The “ aural system” of teaching the deaf
and dumb is carried to great perfection just now
in England. I was at the opera the other night
with a deaf young lady, and between the acts 1
addressed a remark to her, to which she replied—
“ Hush ! don’t disturb me. That gentleman in
the box opposite has just proposed to that
lady, and I am watching her lips to see
what she replies.” And what does she reply?”
I continued in a few minutes. “She says,”
replied my astute companion, “ that she is secretly
married already, and has a boy of two.” The
worst of these deaf people is that they can’t judge
how loud they talk themselves, and no sooner had
my friend yelled out the -words “ a boy of two ”
than I perceived to my horror that the gentleman
and lady opposite had caught the sounds. Shortly
after that they left the box, but not together.—
Akgus London Correspondent.
A Wairarapa (N.Z.) justice fined himself
lately: in the sum of £5 17s. for a breach of the
Rabbit Act. It was evident from the astonished
expression of his countenance that he had expected
a smaller sentence, and he was just rising to pro-
test when he ordered himself to sit down. Then
there was a lively struggle between himself in his
private capacity and the other part of him, and
the language he used was so violent that he
ordered himself to leave the court instantly, and
when he refused to go, and said he would see him-
self dashed first, and used disrespectful language
to himself, he got so mad that he rushed down
from the bench and started to throw himself out.
11l the combat which ensued he struck himself
several times and got in heavily on his nose, but
ultimately he lifted himself bodily by the hair and
threw him—in his private capacity—down the
steps and left him partially stunned and using
strong language on the pavement. Then he re-
turned and went on with the business till, after-
wards hearing himself still cursing in the street,
he sent the constable out to warn him to desist,
but that officer presently returned and stated that
he had been unable to find him. He thqn in-
structed the sergeant of police to keep an eye on
him, and in case of any further disturbance to rush
in between him and drag Him apart, and he further
added that if he saw himself doing anything of
the sort a?ain he would fine both of him. A cool-
ness has sprung up between him in consequence of
these events, and that night when he was going
to bed lie assaulted himself violently when he
wasn’t looking, and hit himself a severe blow from
behind.
The high-priest of Venus Cloacena at
Kiama, -N.S.W., nominated himself for an alder-
manship lately. He stood a good show, but
withdrew on finding that if elected he would be
divorced from his nightly occupation.

FREETRADE or PROTECTION.
Some vote Protection, some Freetrade,
As best for this young country’s weal.
But ask the youth or blushing maid,
And each will answer— Pemell’s Meal I
As best, because it has been tried,
There’s nothing half so good beside.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap, I wean.

When Jefferson, the actor, came from
America to Australia, he had a continual wrangle
on the vessel with Father O’Grady, a priest, on
the subject of celibacy. They parted in Sydney,
where they re-met in three years’time. O’Grady
had left the Church and got married. However,
he was on the best of terms with the old establish-
ment.

Two Days’ Wages.
Part I.— His First Day’s Wages.
Tramp, tramp, tramp!
Two men came slowly and carefully along the
passage of a London hospital, carrying a stretcher
between them. An open window let in the cold
evening air and the deafening noise of the street ;
but they heeded not these things, neither did the
stifling atmosphere of the theatre seem to affect
their senses. The theatre door stood ajar, and
the men cast a glance at the operation-table as
they walked on with their burden. They came to
the door of a ward, and halted for a minute. One
of them bent down, and lifted up a dark-brown
blanket that lay across the stretcher.
“ I’ve a little lad at home no bigger than this
one ” he said, apologetically to his companion.
“ He can’t be more than twelve, I should say, to
look at him. My little lad was eleven last Christ-
-10 “ What is it ?” inquired the other, using hospital
jargon.
“Can’t say,” was the answer. “He was run
' over by a cab while selling newspapers in the
last speaker then replaced the blanket,
opened the ward-door, and called out “Number
six!” . .
There was a stir amongst the patients. Men
turned their heads towards the door, wondering
who lay on the stretcher. Number seven fixed his
languid eyes on the brown blanket. Number five
stopped playing chess with Number 20, and leant
on his elbow to watch what would happen. Then
came a hush of expectation, broken by the groans
of the newspaper-boy and the voice of the hos-
pital sister, who was giving orders to the nurses.
A screen was placed round the bed, almost before
the men could see the face of the new patient, but
Number five caught a glimpse of the little lad, and
he rubbed his forehead thoughtfully as he asked,
“ Whose move is it?”
The evening wore away, but the screen was not
moved from the bed of Number six. Doctors
came into the ward, and disappeared behind the
screen, the nurses looked grave, and the sister
seemed anxious. So the patients guessed that
Number six was a bad case ; and a few words from
the house-surgeon confirmed their suspicions.
“ Have you found out anything about him,
sister ? ” the young man asked.
“Nothing.”
“ Sorry for that. He won’t live till morning.”
The doctor then walked towards the sister’s
room, saying something about “a special” for
Number six.
Before long the gas was turned low, and every-
thing was put in order for the night. Afterwards
the ward was very quiet. Nothing disturbed the
patients but an occasional groan from a man who
could not sleep, or the cry of someone who had
started up from a nightmare, and had found his
limbs tightly bandaged. The peculiar odour that
tells of surgical operations grew stronger as the
hours wore on, and the breath of twenty men
made the air heavy, although the windows were
partly open. Inside the ward the patients could
distinguish the night nurse’s footstep; outside
they could only hear the dull roar of distant carts
and voices. Now and then a railway-whistle
broke the stillness; rarer still was the nurse’s
voice as she moved about among the patients.
A “ special ” sat beside the bed of Number six ;
but she was hidden by the screen, and if the night
nurse had not sometimes spoken to her, in a
whisper, the patients would not have been aware
of her presence.
The hospital clock struck eleven.
The last stroke had scarcely fallen when a
woman pushed open the door of the ward. She
advanced slowly, with uncertain steps, and stood
quite still when she saw the nurse.
“ They told me I should find ray boy here,” she
said, in a low, frightened voice. “He’s not bad,
is he? He’s but little to go out selling papers,
but I’ve fourlittler than he, and the baby’s ill, and
we’ve nothing in the house.”
The nurse looked at the thin, worn face of the
mother, and then towards the screen that hid the
bed of Number Six.
“Your boy was run over in the street,” she
said.
“Yes, ma’am,” said the woman, “they told me
about it downstairs. I looked everywhere for him
after dark, and the policeman sent me here.
Where is he? Ever since his father died he’s been
wanting to help me and talking about what he’d
do when he got his first day’s wages. Let me gto
to him, ma’am. If anything happens to him it
will break my heart; where is he ?”
Before the nurse could answer, the screen was
pushed aside and the “special” came towards
them saying,
“ The boy is dead.”
“ Dead !” echoed the mother, “ dead !”
She stumbled to the screen and fell on her knees
beside the bed.
“So this is your first day’s wages, my darling,”
she said.
Pakt ll.— His Last Day’s Wages.
“Hail, Mary, full of grace ; blessed art thou
amongst women, and blessed is the fruit of thy
womb.”
The words were repeated again and again by a
man who was kneeling in a corner of a little
Catholic church in the East-end of London.
The beads dropped one by one through the
man’s fingers, and his lips moved mechanically
until he came to his last “Hail, Mary.”. Then
he bowed his head, and made the sign oif the cross.
The church was dimly lighted, so it was impos-
sible to see his face; but his tall, lean figure was
visible, and his iron-grey hair. Presently he
picked up an old hat that lay on the floor beside
him, and made his way to a crucifix. Then he
knelt down again.
“ God have mercy on me,” he prayed. “ Mother
of God, have mercy on my little children ! ”
After that he left the church. He went to a
bread-shop, bought a loaf, and went on home.
His face was grey with hunger, but he said to
himself that he was scarcely hungry, only foot-
sore and tired. The loaf mustdo for the children’s
supper and for the children’s breakfast.
It had not always been with him like this. He
could remember the day when he walked proudly
along the streets, feeling sure of employment. But
times had changed since then, and now he was
out of work. Moreover, his wife had been driven
into the lunatic asylum by hunger and trouble,
leaving him with four little boys and a baby girl
that must be clothed, housed, and fed. He was
father and mother in one to five children, the
youngest of whom was but a baby. His wife had
lost her reason after her last confinement; and
since then all things had seemed to go wrong with
him.
A few Fours later he was sitting in a small room,
lighted by a-dip candle. On the table was half of
the loaf —the children’s break r ast. Beside him, in
a wooden cradle, was his youngest child, the one
that had cosf his wife her reason. He could hear
it breathing. The other boys-lay on an old mat-
tress, fast asleep, but restless. One of them said,
“Father!” and he rose up to see what the child
wanted. But by the time he reached the bed,
and bent down, shading the candle with his hand,
the boy was asleep again. He stood watching the
children, and while he looked a spasm of pain
contorted his face. He threw a hungry glance, at
the bread on the table—the children’s breakfast ;
then he snuffed out the candle with his fingers,
drew the leather belt tighter round his waist, and
lay down on the mattress. He could not sleep,
for hunger was gnawing his stomach; but he
listened to the children breathing, and he waited
for daybreak.
Half-past seven o’clock found him at the London
Docks. It was a month since lie had been
“ taken on ” there ; but he came there every morn-
ing, and when he found that he con’d not get a job
there, he went to look for work in the market.
In he went, through the gates, with the rest;
and this time lie was taken on, A smile passed
over his grey face as he straightened himself, and
followed the other men. But he felt his legs
shaking under him, and the ships seemed to be
dancing up and clown. The sun, that was rising
like a red ball in the east, danced before his eyes.
He rubbed his sleeve against his forehead and
staggered on, saying nothing to anyone. Once he
very nearly fell into the water and only saved
himself by catching hold of an tlvr man, who
called him “a bloody idiot,” which was meant as
a term of endearment. The smile came over his
face again. That night ho would give the chil-
dren a treat; they should have more than bread
for their supper.
No one seemed to guess that no food had passed
his lips for almost a week. He worked on in
silence, and, when the dinner-bell rang, he stag-
gered to a lonely plage while the other men ate
their dinner. Orders seemed to travel to him from
a long distance, and his movements were so un-
steady that the other men laughed, thinking that
he must be tipsy. But he managed to do his work,
and the foreman did not grumble.
At last it was time to go homo.
Jostled by the other men, lie went through the
warehouses, and arrived at the pay-house. Ho
held out his hand for the money, and, having
received it,die slipped the shillings into his empty
pocket.
“Pass on,” said a voice beh'nd him.
“Make way,” cried someone.
“Pick him up,” exchi’med half-a-dozen dockers.
“He’s dead, or fainted.”
He had fallen on the ground, in front of the pay-
house, fallen with a heavy thud, just after slipping
the shillings into his pocket.
“Call a policeman,” suggested a man.
Two tall policemen lifted him up and carried
him through the dock-gates to the nearest coffee-
shop. A crowd of dockers followed him. Was
he drunk ? Was he dead ? Had he fainted ? No
one could tell for certain. He was laid oh a table
in the coffee-house and guarded by a policeman,
until the nearest doctor could be fetched. Mean-
while they emptied his pockets, hoping to find his
address. But the pockets only held a few shillings.
The money was counted and placed on the table.
“May I never see a corpse again if he’s not
dead,” remarked the mast: r of the coffee-house.
“Make room for the doctor,” cried several
voices.
“He’s dead, sir,” explained the policeman.
“ Allow me to see for myself,” said the doctor.
They unbuckled the leather-belt that had kept
famine at bay, and the doctor made his examina-
tion with a professional air, talking to himself,
taking no notice of the policeman, or of the master
of the coffee-house.
“ Yes, the man is dead,” he said at last,
“Heart-disease,” suggested the master of the
coffee-house.
“No, starvation,” said the doctor, grimly.
‘ Whose coin is this ? ” he continued, taking two
shillings from the table, and laying them on the
docker’s closed eyelids.
“It’s the dead man’s money, sir,” said the
policeman —“ his last day’s wages.”
J.L.— In Pall Mall Gazette.
An English syndicate, capital £lO,OOO, has
been formed, under the auspices of one Calvert, to
cut put an alleged 4|-millioh gold-deposit in West
Australia. Calvert is going to consign his millions
to the Bank of England. The English can’t be
one of the lost tribes. If they were they’d be less
credulous.
A correspondent of a Sydney daily paper
writes to prove that Joseph Arch, the English
labourer's friend, is a humbug. “I presided,” he
says, “at a public meeting at Ipswich. I heard
Mr. Arch receive cheers when he vowed no man
could beat him at farming work, and ho drank no
beer. A few hours later on as he was going to
bed he asked mo to oblige him by securing a bottle
of port wine, and by taking it to his bedroom.
He felt so depressed he must have some more.”
Judge Chubb, of Queensland, came down
to the” court, the other day, with smothered
thunder in his aspect and dashed himself down on
the bench in a bloodthirsty way which suggested
that he was about to throw himself bodily at
somebody, and when the court was duly opened,
this irritated man made a statement. It then
transpired that some ignorant clod on horseback
had come up to his house at a gallop and had
hollered out: “Does Chubb live here?” Being
informed that Judge Chubb resided in a judicial
manner on these premises and was accessible when
approached in a gentlemanly fashion, the soulless
mass of dirt then threw a letter in a disrespectful
sort of way on the verandah, and immediately
afterwards removed his offensive carcase off the
landscape. His manners throughout seemed
to imply that ho didn’t care a hang, and
after seeing him Judge Chubb naturally felt
that he would prefer to have . his letters
delivered in future by a gorilla. Or, if the
gorilla wasn’t handy, the correspondence could
be brought round by a naked cannibal, and failing
that any person who wished to communicate with
him and didn’t know enough to prevent him
from shrieking for plain “ Chubb” in the
street had better employ a hog or some other
animal with a sense of propriety about it, as an
intermediary. Further, apart from the galloping
and shrieking, it was highly irregular that any
alleged human being who seemed to have been
educated on an ash-heap should dash the judge’s
correspondence down on any verandah, and then
depart as if he thought that judicial functionary
had got hydrophobia, and was likely to rush after
him and bite him. The whole proceeding was
eminently disrespectful, and it was .hoped: that a,
repetition would be avoided, otherwise something
of a sudden nature might take place when it was
least expected.

A Rig-lit Royal Toast.—A Story of Maori
Hospitality.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ The eminent scientist, Dr. Blubber, has
just given to the world his long-looked-for work,
‘The Birds of New Zealand,”’ announced the
London correspondent of a daily paper some days
ago. “ It is magnificently illustrated in colours and
only a limited number have been printed. The
work appears in three vols., beautifully-bound,
the price being ten guineas. Excepting a few
copies reserved by the author, the entire publica-
tion has already been sold. Her Majesty the
Queen has been presented with a copy.”
At last, thought I, “ The Birds of New Zea-
land,” in face of many obstacles, has seen the
light. I meant the book, for the birds about
which it was written had seen the light, many of
them, perhaps, long before I did. Some years ago,
in New Zealand, I had met Dr. Blubber when he
was preparing the material for his great work, and
our acquaintance was made about the time when
one of the obstacles or reverses of which I have
spoken occurred.
It was in the month of May, some eight or nine
years ago—l really forget the year ; but it was
about the time when, after some 20 years of isola-
tion, the Kingite Maoris had agreed to break
down the barrier of reserve and mix freely with
the Europeans across the border.
To fittingly celebrate the occasion and to
formally throw open the King country—that vast
tract of beautiful land which extends from the
slopes of stately Pirongia and the majestic
heights of Maungatautara right through the
island to the feet of Egmont itself —to mark with
fitting ceremony this great event, King Tawhiao
convened what he called his “ Great May Meet-
ing,” to be held on the banks of the Waipa in the
royal township of Whatiwhatihoe. Across the
river and under the shadow of the great mountain
nestled Alexandra, ultima thule of civilisation.
Here during the war the British forces had formed
their most advanced outposts and built the last
redoubt of a long line extending from Auckland
tothePinui River. There was—and is now for
aught I know —a solitary hotel in the settlement
presided over by one Tom Einch—Goldfinch we
used to call him, from a desire on his part to
always get the cash in advance. Here gathered,
at the first intimation of the opening of the Kins
Country, the usual herd of vultures who in variou.
ways swoop down on the Maori, endow him with
what they call “advantages of civilisation,” and
by way of compensation nibble up his heritage to
the last acre. The land of the King country was
supposed to be of the best, the hills were rich in
gold; the very finest timber was alleged to exist in
the dense forests, coal abounded, and the deep
forest glades were reputed to be alive with the
rarest and most beautiful birds.
So at Tom Finch’s hostelry were lodged land-
sharks, gold-hunters, speculators, newspaper cor-
respondents, ornithologists, jobbers, lawyers, as
varied and representative a lot of the “ pioneers of
civilisation ” as ever gathered under one roof. The
accommodation being limited, the lion had to lie
down with the lamb, and as I was one of the
latter, the adage was fulfilled in my case by having
to sandwich myself on a gigantic shake-down
between an attorney and a Mormon missionary.
We were all there waiting the permission to ad-
vance ; and it was while so doing that I first made
tire acquaintance of the author of the “Birds of
New Zealand.”
“ j am going into the bush to-morrow,” he said
to me one night; “ I’m going to get a few speci-
mens of birds for my collection. As you know, 1
am compiling my second work on the ‘ Birds of
New Zealand, ’ and as many of our best specimens
are dyj n g ou * iam naturally anxious to get what
i can for my mU seum as soon as possible. The
bush around Pirongia, I am told, contains a good
representative collection ; and so I’ll pitch my
camp there for a wee k 0 r two. ”
“ Alone ? ’I asked.
“Practically,” he’replied, “unless you favour
me with your’company, if you feel interested in
work like mine.”
\ was flighted with the opportunity. The next
morning our camp equipage was packed on a
couple of horses; our guides set out in front to
lead the way ; and before nightfall w 6 pitched our
tent in the heart of the forest beside a little brook-
let, surrounded by Nature in all her beauty and
freshness. The thousand little songsters extended
us their warmest greeting; and the mosquitoes
gave us a welcome which, in any other country,
must surely be reserved for royalty,
As tq, e plumage of the birds had to be re-pro-
duced on costly plates, and the birds preserved for
presentation to the colonial and British museums,
our first duty was to erect a temporary aviary out
of the material at hand, and this completed, we
went to work in earnest. For days we laboured
incessantly, setting snares and trapping our game,
Every day added different specimens to our collec-
tion ; and the utmost care had to be taken that
our little prisoners did not destroy each other,
The doctor was gratified beyond his fondest anti-
cipations. For over a fortnight we toiled together
in that wild solitude, dragging our weary limbs
along through the dense undergrowth. Wet or
dry our work proceeded. At the end of the second
week, when we gazed at our collection, our ecstasy
knew no bounds. All the most beautiful birds for
which the island colony was remarkable were re-
presented in our collection. The only thing to be
done was to get them to a place of security ; and
this was an undertaking which required great
care. We ultimately decided to leave our Maori
boy in charge while we went into Alexandra to
order cages. Our clothes were badly torn, our
limbs scratched and lacerated, and the constant
exposure to the wet bush had brought on colds
which, in others less strongly constituted, might
have terminated in a galloping consumption,
m * *
ri , . ... ~ , m u-
was about this time that King lawhiao
announced his great bird-feast, to which all the
leading Europeans of the colony were invited,
Special invitations were sent to the Governor, the
Ministry, the Mayors of boroughs, and all other
functionaries. A special invitation was also
extended to Dr. Blubber and myself. We were
informed that the King’s cooks had been out on a
shooting and trapping expedition in the ranges for
some days, and that the bird-feast would be the
grandest ever given in the records of IS ew Zealand
royalty.
The bird-feast, as it was called, was a purely
royal institution. Some hundreds of birds of
various kinds, mostly pigeons, were plucked and
cleaned and then treated to the steaming process
known as “Orapu Maori.” When done in this
fashion, birds, whether carrion or spring chicken,
are a dish fit “to lay before a king.” The flesh
falls from the frame in rich, juicy shreds, and the
small bones make a succulent and dainty morsel.
On arriving at our hotel we found the invitations
awaiting us. As we wished to ingratiate ourselves
into the good graces of Tawhiao, we accepted the
invitation to the feast without hesitation. The
following morning, dressed in our best, we set out
in state on tWo borrowed horses, and, with a
number of other “distinguished Europeans ” made
a kind of triumphal entry into the royal township
of Whatiwhatihoe. Our way was directed to the
Senate-house known as the “Rununga,” where
we were formally received by his Majesty, who,
in compliment to his guests, wore trousers for the
occasion. The royal standard (which I sub;e-
quently ascertained v. as the Queen’s petticoat)
floated in the breeze above; and when I was pre-
sented to his Majesty, and made my profoundest
bow, I felt that the proudest moment of my life
was at hand.
Dinner with Royalty ! My happiness knew no
limits. Come death itself, what cared I for the
terrors of the next world ! I had attained the
acme of happiness in this.
The bell rang, the korero ceased, and out filed
the guests to the bird-feast on the lawn without. I
escorted on my arm the Princess Mere Whatatutu,
while the doctor had the extreme honour of bring-
ing along the Queen, the fair and graceful Pohaere,
neatly attired in a mackintosh and a boxer-hat.
As the Maoris bidden to the feast outnumbered
the Europeans by about twenty to one, our opera-
tions at the festive board were confined to a squint
at the savoury heap through the legs of our enter-
tainers. Though we had come prepared with
whetted appetites we didn’t complain.
At last the scramble ended, and the Doctor was
called upon to propose the health of the King,
who by this time had discarded his trousers for
a kind of primitive kilt.
“ We have assembled here to-day,” said the
little Doctor, inflated as much with his own im-
portance as with the magnitude of the occasion;
“ we are met here to-day, gentlemen, to do honour
to our friend Tawhiao, and to celebrate a great
political epoch, the opening of the King country.
(Cheers.) His Majesty has provided us with a
most excellent feast, which I for one have greatly
enjoyed ; and for which I, on your behalf, beg to
return him our most sincere thanks. (Loud
cheers.) The dish of which we have this day par-
taken proves that in the birds of New Zealand, we
have not only exquisite beauty but also rare
quality and flavour. (Great cheering.) Gentle-
men, t give you ‘The King.’” (Loud and pro-
longed cheering.)
His Majesty was then drunk (quite drunk), and
the company, perfectly pleased with itself, pro-
ceeded to break up.
* * *
“IsayTui, where did you get all those
delicious birds that you cooked for the feast ? ”
asked the doctor of an aged Maori as we wended
our way through the royal demesne.
“Kapai—Maori plenty of birds,” said the
ancient. “ Any amount in the bush. Maori man
get ’em plenty of nice fat pigeons, plenty tui,
plenty kiwi, plenty weka.”
“Do you know the place?” asked the doctor,
growing quite agitated with the prospect of
another haul.
“Yes, yes”—our ancient knew the very spot.
He could bring us there in less than no time. We
offered to pay handsomely if he would take us to
the place to-morrow. Yes, he was agreeable. He
would show the pakeha rangatiras where there was
a perfect mine of wealth of the rarest specimens.
The following morning Tui kept his appoint-
ment. At daybreak we started with all our
available snaring paraphernalia and provisions for
two days. Into the bush we dived; and on
through the semi-darkness we went. Should we
never come to the place ? At last we halted. The
country seemed familiar. The doctor was on pins
and needles, eager for work. His eyes twinkled
with visions of the bagful which wo were to carry
away to swell his museum collections and illus-
trate his book. Happy, happy doctor !
Down an open ravine we dive, the horses
scarcely able to keep their feet, and on we press
over the opposite ridge through the dense under-
growth. At last we strike a beaten track and
press quickly forward.
“Why, we’re on the old beat !” I ventured to
remark as, turning in the saddle, I am confronted
with the white face of the doctor, full of mis-
givings.
“I think so,” he replied, with a sickening
attempt at a smile.
The next minute the guide brought us right in
front of our own aviary, which we had erected with
such pains and filled with the proceeds of our
fortnight’s industry.
The Maori boy we had left in charge had de-
serted ; the wattle-door had been burst open ; and
not a single feather was to be seen anywhere. Our
fortnight’s work had gone for nothing !
“There !”said our guide, pointing his big black
finger at the ruin in front of us ; “ Plenty of birds;
any amount.” He had the audacity to extend his
hand for the reward.
I tried to smile, but the attempt was a failure ;
the doctor coughed and turned away ; while the
native filled in the interval with a little musical
levity, which, under the circumstances, was rank
sacrilege.
There was no doubting the fact —it was palpable.
The King’s cooks in search of material for their
bird-feast had stumbled across our collection and
had annexed it bodily. But what was more and
what was worse, we of all people had helped to eat
it and had actually returned thanks for being
allowed to do so.
“ Wasn’t it sickenin’? ”
J. F. EDGAR.

Dear Bulletin, —That little story about
Dibbs is neither new nor true. It was told about
a greater man than Mr. Dibbs—viz., Bismarek,
who, when the Germans entered Paris, was
frowned on by the few who recognised him, and
just in a spirit of fun, or perhaps dare-devilry,
went over to one of the crowd and asked for a
match. It might never have happened, in Bis-
march’s career any more than it did in Dibbs’,
but the story is told in a recent number of the
Nineteenth Century magazine by Blenner-
hasset.—A Bui lbtin Reader. [Bismarck pro-
bably borrowed it from Dibbs. Or, perhaps, each
did it independently of the other. Geniuses often
jump together. —Ed. B.]

Late House Surgeon to the Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous Complaints.
De. PERRY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in consequence of the number
of persons suffering from diseases of this nature remaining uncured ; and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, because the majority of persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DULY QUALIFIED MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
oy a practitioner who has gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
Anatomical Training in the best hospitals ; and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet he is promoting the usefulness and thk dignity of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease,
than by leaving them severely alone to the care of men who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
medical training to become acquainted with the best methods of treating
these complex forms of disease.

AT the CLARKNDCXN HOTEL, Banquets, Suppers, Private Dinners, Evening Parties, etc., are
served on the Shortest Notice in the Large Dining Hall. Besides having a most recherche
Dinner, you can enjoy from the balconies the magnificent view of the sea and surrounding country;
here also you can have good company, good wines—in fact, everything that can possibly make one
comfortable can be found at this really first-class hotel. Fishing and Picnic Parties provided. Table
de Hole at One o’clock daily and Sundays. No expense is spared by Mrs. Kilminstek to make her
visitors comfortable and her Hotel the beat in the colony.
